University Of Copenhagen (UCPH). The project was conducted in the Department of Health 
Technology in the Colloids & Biological Interface (CBIO) Group. This project is credited

Abstract 
NK cells play a pivotal role in the control of tumor growth and elimination, and during the 
past decades they have been investigated as a potent tool in cancer therapy. In contrast to T 
cells, NK cells do not need prior sensitization to exert their anti-tumor activity. Just like every 
other cell, NK cells communicate with their surrounding cells, with their relationship with 
neutrophils being an important part in the anti-tumor immune response.  Many approaches 
have been employed to activate NK cells in vivo, predominantly by administering activating 
cytokines like IL-12. This study evaluates the effect of IL-12 immunotherapy in modulating 
tumor growth and metastasis in vivo from an NK cell and neutrophil point of view.  
Our primary hypothesis was that a TrIL-12 immunotherapeutic agent would activate NK cells 
to exert a tumoricidal response. IL-12 administration to 4T1 bearing mice indeed delayed 
primary tumor growth without any signs of significant adverse effects. Moreover, the IL-12 
based immunotherapy regimen appeared to exert an anti-metastatic effect as seen by the 
reduction in metastatic lesions in the lungs. To translate these observations into the immune 
cell distribution, we generated various flow cytometry panels to phenotype the cells in the 
TME and the lungs. This way we identified distinct NK cell and neutrophil populations. Our 
observations led us to the discovery that IL-12 modulated pro-tumorigenic Siglec-Fhigh 
neutrophils, and possible skewed neutrophils towards a pro-inflammatory state. We also 
assessed the functional input of neutrophils in the primary tumor and the lungs, illuminating 
the role of the neutrophil subsets during tumor progression. Furthermore, NK cells displayed 
features of activation and effector functions as measure by IFN-g, GzmB, CD25, TIM-3 and 
TIGIT expression. Finally, we were able to get a better understanding regarding the 
relationship of NK cells and neutrophils, and make an association between their balance and 
the lung metastasis. Due to the aggressiveness of the 4T1 tumor cells we could not draw a 
clear conclusion on the effect of IL-12 based immunotherapy on the overall survival of the 
mice.  
Despite the effectiveness of IL-12 immunotherapy in modulating tumor metastasis, more 
studies need to be done to fully harness its true potential. Future studies should focus on 
combining this immunotherapeutic with agents that modulate neutrophils and NK cells, or 
compounds that can affect the viability of tumor cells. Combination of these therapies with 
DC or T cells transfer could have a synergistic effect and stronger anti-tumor potential, and as 
such they should also be investigated. TrIL-12 could be combined with adoptive transfer of 
NK cells, potentiating both the host’s and the donor’s cells.  It would be wise to explore the 
full functional imprint of the neutrophil populations and the role of extracellular vesicles as 
by understanding the full extent of their tumor modulating potential could lead to the 
discovery of more potent and targeted therapies.

1. Introduction 
The host’s defense against various pathogens and malignancies is governed by the immune 
system, which is divided into the innate and the adaptive immune system. The innate 
immunity is mediated by myeloid and some lymphoid cells, while the adaptive immunity is 
controlled by lymphoid cells [1]. Albeit separate, there is a communication link between the 
two systems to ensure the fine tuning of the immune response. As part of the innate 
immunity, natural killer (NK) cells are the first line sentinels, looking for transformed cells 
that could potentially lead to disease. NK cells were originally discovered in mice in 1975 
from total splenocytes as cells that could eliminate the target cancer or virus-infected cell [1, 
2]. They are a population of large lymphocytes containing granules with cytotoxic mediators, 
capable of exerting their cytotoxic functions without priming a priori [3, 4]. Unlike T cells 
and B cells, NK cells do not undergo somatic recombination to alter their receptor repertoire. 
Instead, they have a set of germline encoded receptors by which they recognize “self” from 
“non-self” molecules. There are both activating and inhibitory receptors mediating this 
recognition [3, 5]. After activation, NK cells utilize an arsenal of killing mechanisms (directly 
cytotoxic or indirect cytotoxicity-immune modulating) in order to eliminate their target [4]. 
As they do not need external immunization to act, NK cells have become the target of interest 
of many researchers in hopes of exploiting their killing potential against cancer [6]. Indeed, 
the implementation of NK cells as a form of immunotherapy (both as a monotherapy of a 
combinational therapy) has yielded a lot of attention and produced some promising results 
against various malignancies, also partially because of the minimized risks and side effects 
observed compared to chemotherapy and radiotherapy, which constitute the current standard 
of care [6]. 
1.2 Natural Killer Cells 
1.2.1 Origin & Development of NK cells 
NK cells correspond to a population of innate lymphoid cells (ILC), originating from the bone 
marrow (BM) in both humans and mice [4, 7]. Indeed, the BM is the cradle of immune cell 
generation and development. Despite the fact that BM is the primary NK cell generator, their 
maturation can be concluded in secondary tissues like the lymph nodes, the liver and the 
uterus [3]. NK cells arise from the hematopoietic stem cell (HSC) niches in the BM (Figure 
1). The cytokine milieu favors the development of the immune cell progenitors while 
maintaining the HSC pool [2, 4]. Once stimulated by the appropriate cytokines, the 
CD34+CD117+Stem Cell Antigen (Sca)+. HSCs give rise to the Common Lymphoid 
Progenitors (CLPs). These progenitor cells constitute the generators of all the lymphoid 
lineages [4]. Within the CLPs, there is a distinct population expressing cluster of 
differentiation (CD) CD122 (widely known as the Interleukin (IL)-2 receptor chain β), which 
will give rise to the first stage of cells committed to the NK cell lineage, the NK cell 
precursors (NKP) and the common helper innate lymphoid progenitor (CHILP), that will 
generate the rest of the tissue resident innate lymphoid cells [4, 7-9]. For their commitment to 
the NK lineage, the CD122+ CLPs are highly dependent on IL-2 and mostly IL-15 [4, 7], 
since silencing of their respective receptors, results in absence of NK cells [7].  Both IL-2 and 
IL-15 work synergistically with IL-12 to induce the proliferation, survival and differentiation 
of NK cells [10]. Moving forward, NKP generates the first stage of immature NK cells (iNK). 
During this developmental stage, iNK cells express some but not all of the markers commonly 
found on the terminally differentiated and developed mature NK cells (mNK) [4, 8, 11]. 
Several markers have been identified in the iNK state, such as CD27, CD11b, CD226, L-
Selectin and some transcription factors like the E protein antagonist ID2 (ID2) and T-box 
protein 2 (T-bet) [2, 4, 11, 12]. Acquisition of activating and inhibitory receptors like the 
==PAGE:9==
[4] 
 
Ly49 family, killer-cell lectin-like receptor G1 (KLRG1), natural cytotoxicity receptors 
(NCR), dictates the transition from the iNK to the mNK phenotype [2, 4, 8]. In this stage, NK 
cells start expressing CD49b, and upregulate CD11b as surface markers, while engaging the 
regulation of specific transcription factors like Eomesodermin (Eomes), Kruppel-like factor 2 
(KLF2) and GATA binding protein 3 (GATA3), with simultaneous downregulation of 
Forkhead box O1 (FOXO1) and CD27 [2]. In this context, CD11b+/highCD27- NK cells 
represent the mature cytotoxic population capable of killing its target [12]. A combination of 
CD11b and CD27 is used to distinguish four sequential developmental stages: CD11b-
/lowCD27-/low, 
CD11blowCD27+/high, 
CD11b+/highCD27+/high 
and 
CD11bhighCD27low 
[13]. 
However, NK cells only represent a fraction of the ILCs. Over the years, several studies 
showed a certain degree of diversity in this group, hence further dividing them in separate 
populations namely ILC group 1 (ILC1), ILC group 2 (ILC2) and ILC group 3 (ILC3) [14]. 
NK cells belong to ILC1, and they are distinguished from the rest of the cells in that group 
due to their cytotoxic ability and certain phenotypic differences, compared to the other ILC1s, 
which have a helper function [14, 15]. ILC group 2 and 3 are not cytotoxic, but cytokine 
producing (helper innate lymphoid cells), mediating different functions in the tissues they 
reside [14, 16].

As mentioned above, NK cells can continue their development in secondary tissues. In the 
liver, NK cells are characterized as CD49b-, compared to their circulating counterparts that 
are CD49b+ [13]. Those tissue resident NK cells also express different chemokine receptors 
during the course of their developmental maturation. As an example, NKP cells express high 
levels of the C-X-C motif Chemokine Receptor 4 (CXCR4), which is downregulated as the 
cell matures, followed by the upregulation of CXCR3 [13]. CXCR3 is also present on the NK 
cells residing in the liver [13]. Finally, chemokine receptors are necessary for the migration of 
cells in response to chemokine gradients. In mice, CXCR3 is expressed from the immature 
stage until the mature stage and facilitates the NK cell migration via C-X-C motif chemokine 
ligand 9 (CXCL10), CXCL10 and CXCL11 [17]. Wendel et al. reported that exogenous 
administration of CXCL10 or tumors engineered to express this CXCR3 ligand, yielded more 
CXCR3+ NK cells in the tumor and prolonged their survival [18]. The importance of this 
chemokine receptor in NK cell migration towards tumor sites is highlighted by the fact that 
CXCR3 deficient NK cells fail to migrate or migrate slower in response to CXCL10 stimuli, 
as demonstrated in a melanoma B16F10 model [19].   
==PAGE:10==
[5] 
 
 
Figure 1 Development of Natural Killer cells in the bone marrow. A schematic overview of the 
developmental process occurring in the BM, starting from the HSC until the finally differentiated mature 
NK cell. The HSC pool in the BM gives rise to the CLP population which will differentiate into the CILP. 
From this stage, two precursor populations will arise, the NKP and the CILP. The NKP will differentiate 
into the immature NK cells, which upon maturation will develop into the fully functional mature NK cells. 
From the same CILP the differentiated CHILP population will generate the three groups of innate 
lymphoid cells; ILC1, ILC2 and ILC3.  
Like the murine NK cells, human NK cells undergo a similar developmental process, starting 
with the development in the BM, and just like in mice, expression of CD122 irreversibly 
commits the CLP into the NK cell line [4]. By responding to IL-15, human NK cells 
characterized as CD3-CD56+ upregulate CD94 and develop to the CD56bright phenotype, hence 
it is used to characterize various stages during NK cell development [20]. Subsequent 
downregulation of CD94 and expression of CD16 and killer receptors marks the mature state 
of NK cells, known as the CD56dim NK cell population, which in juxtaposition to the murine 
CD11b+CD27- population is the most cytotoxic one [20, 21]. Interestingly, evidence suggests 
that NK cells may also originate from myeloid precursors, and to be more specific from 
granulocytic-monocytic precursors (GMP), as it was shown that isolation of those cells from 
cord blood could develop into functional NK cells, with the addition of the appropriate NK 
cytokine cocktail [21]. On the transcriptional level, upregulation of T-bet and simultaneous 
downregulation of Eomes marks the transition from the CD56brightCD16- to CD56dimCD16+ 
phenotype, with the expression of KIRs [3, 22, 23].  
1.2.2 Activation & Effector functions of NK cells 
NK cells play an important role not only as defenders against abnormal or infected cells, but 
also as homeostatic mediators. Unlike T and B cells that have a wide repertoire of receptors 
that are developed after antigen recognition [24], NK cells possess an arsenal of germline 
encoded receptors mediating their activation [25, 26]. Compared to lymphocytes of the 
adaptive immunity that require antigen presentation through major-histocompatibility 
complex (MHC), NK cells recognize ligands expressed on abnormal cells through their 
activating and inhibitory receptors [27, 28]. Those stress molecules can bind either activating 
or inhibitory receptors. Whether the NK cell is activated or inhibited is dictated by the fine 
balance between those signals, termed the net signal (Figure 2) [25, 29, 30]. The inhibitory 
receptors have a cytoplasmic tail of immunoreceptor tyrosine-based inhibition motifs (ITIM), 
while the activating ones bare a cytoplasmic tail of immunoreceptor tyrosine-based activation 
motifs (ITAM) [26, 28, 31].      
==PAGE:11==
[6] 
 
 
Figure 2 Protein families of NK cell activating and inhibitory receptors mediating their activation. The 
activation of the NK cells is governed by a plethora of signals, both activating and inhibitory. Those signals 
are transduced by different receptors expressed on the surface of the NK cells. Depending of the nature of 
the net signal forwarded by those receptors, the NK cell will either be activated or inhibited. Some of the 
activating receptors include the NKG2D, NKp46, the NKG2C/CD94 complex, CD16 which me diated the 
ADCC in response to antibody recognition, the 2B4 receptor and the Ly9 and Ly49 families of receptors. On 
the other hand, the arsenal of inhibitory receptors include TIM-3 and TIGIT, the KLRG-1 which is 
associated with functional exhaustion, the NKG2A/CD94 complex and the inhibitory receptors of the Ly49 
family.  
The activating receptors expressed by NK cells can be divided based on their respective 
ligands e.g. cytokine responsive, MHC class I specific, and MHC-I non-related [24]. Some of 
the most well established activating receptors include CD16, NKG2C/CD94 and NCRs such 
as NKp30, NKp46 [26, 30, 32, 33]. On the other hand, the inhibitory receptors can be roughly 
sub-divided into two groups: these recognizing MCH-I molecules and those that are 
indifferent of MHC-I ligation (like cytokine receptors) [24]. These receptors include the wide 
Ly49 family, NKG2A/CD94, T cell immunoglobulin and ITIM domain (TIGIT), (TIM-3) and 
many more [24, 31, 33-35]. It is noteworthy that not all activating receptors are present at a 
given time on NK cells. Instead, they can be induced by cytokines like IL-2 [28]. Generally, 
the MHC-I responding receptors are inhibitory ones tasked with maintaining the NK cells 
anergic in response to the self-MHC molecules (a homeostatic switch to avoid autoimmune 
reactions) [26, 29, 31]. As far as TIGIT is concerned, it competes with CD226 for CD155 
binding (a ligand for both of those receptors that is expressed by DCs, monocytes and tumor 
cells) [36]. TIGIT+ NK cells displayed higher cytotoxic potential compared to TIGIT-, 
whereas CD155+MHC-I- melanoma cells inhibited that cytotoxic potential [36]. Apart from 
its direct inhibitory effect on NK cells, TIGIT promotes Treg functions, thus helping in the 
establishment of an immunosuppressive environment [34].  
The absence of MHC-I signal on the target cell is the initial spark that will ignite the NK cell 
to eliminate its target. Usually, an infected or a malignant cell will downregulate the MHC-I 
expression [27, 31]. The patrolling NK cells that might come in touch with the MHC-I 
negative cells will establish an immunological synapse that will transduce an activating 
intracellular signal, priming the NK cell towards eliminating the target [26, 31].  This process 
==PAGE:12==
[7] 
 
is termed NK cell education and licensing, and is mediated by the Ly49 family of receptors 
[31, 37, 38]. The main hypothesis in this MHC-I related model of NK cell priming is called 
the “missing-self hypothesis”. This process starts with the development of the NK cells and it 
involves the binding of an inhibitory Ly49 receptor to a self-MHC-I molecule [31, 38]. 
However, not all NK cells express Ly49 receptors, thus they do not undergo licensing.  
 Once the net signal received by the NK cells overpowers that of the inhibitory receptors and 
the synapse is formed, the NK cell can be activated and exert its functions (Figure 3). These 
functions include direct cytotoxicity (granzyme B (GzmB)/ perforin axis, death ligands like 
TNF-related apoptosis-inducing ligand (TRAIL) and Fas ligand (FasL)), or indirect which 
involve the production of cytokines like interferon-γ (IFN-γ), tumor-necrosis factor α (TNF-
α), IL-10, Granulocytic/Monocytic-Colony Stimulating Factor (GM-CSF) [24, 30]. In this 
regard, CD16 plays an important role in direct cell killing. CD16 recognizes IgG antibodies 
bound on the surface of target cells, and upon ligation it activates a process known as 
Antibody Dependent Cellular Cytotoxicity (ADCC) that results into the release of the 
cytotoxic granules found in the NK cells containing GzmB and perforin [39]. Perforin is 
responsible for the formation of pores on the surface of the target cells, which allow the 
entrance of GzmB in the cytoplasm to activate caspase mediated apoptosis [10]. As part of the 
indirect response against malignant cells, the IFN-γ produced by the NK cells can skew the T 
cell responses towards the T helper 1 (Th1) polarization and activate the cytotoxic T cells 
[40]. Finally, NK cells can also recruit dendritic cells (DCs) and in return, DCs enhance the 
cytotoxic activity of NK cells [40].  Regarding TIM-3 and its role in NK cell functionality, 
studies reported that in early stages of tumors, induction of this negative regulator boosts IFN-
γ production by TIM-3+ NK cells, whereas in later stages those NK cells appear to be 
exhausted and low IFN-γ producing [41]. This data suggests that upregulation of TIM-3 
might be an endpoint result of NK cell activation [42]. 
 
Figure 3 NK cell functions. Once the NK cell binds to the MHC-I molecules expressed by the tumor cell 
through its activating receptors, it is capable of lysing the target by a plethora of mechanisms. Those 
mechanisms include membrane bound proteins (death ligands) like the FasL, as well as soluble factors like 
==PAGE:13==
[8] 
 
IFN-γ and TNF-α. Moreover, NK cells possess preformed vesicles packed with granzymes (GzmA, GzmB) 
and perforin, which upon activation they fuse with the plasma membrane and release their content. 
Perforin then forms pores on the surface of the target cell for the granzymes to enter. Once inside the cell, 
GzmA/B will activate the apoptosis cascade leading to the death of the cells.  
1.2.3 NK cells & Cancer Surveillance  
Tumors are cluster of highly proliferating cells harboring mutation in genes associated with 
pivotal biological processes, such as proliferation, apoptosis and growth. In comparison to 
healthy cells, cancer cells often display differential expression of MHC-I molecules, stress-
induced ligands and they present mutated or over-expressed proteins know as tumor-
associated antigens [43]. These alterations can be detected by the immune system through a 
complex process termed cancer immunosurveillance [44]. It is further divided into three 
stages. The first stage is the elimination, during which the immune cells recognize the 
abnormal cells and attack to lyse them. The next stage is called equilibrium, at which there is 
a balance between the cancer cells and the immune cells. During this stage tumors may 
develop new antigens that are not immediately recognized by the immune system and thus 
survive the elimination. Finally, the tumor clusters that survived can escape the 
immunosurveillance (escape stage) and create a tumor-microenvironment (TME) favoring 
their development (Figure 4) [44]. Thus, TME can be seen as a cradle which orchestrates the 
immune-mediated clearance of cancer cells. In fact, TME can be “distinguished” as “hot” or 
“cold”. A “hot” TME is the one that elicits a potent anti-tumor response due to increased 
immune cell infiltration (mainly T cells) and cytokine production, whereas a “cold” TME 
lacks immune cells [45]. In this axis, NK cells have risen to be potent scouts for DC 
recruitment via CCL5 and XCL1; the DC then can attract and re-stimulate cytotoxic T cells 
by secreting IL-12, aiding the development of an antitumor response [46]. The importance of 
the NK/DC axis is highlighted by the experiments showing that depletion of NK cells results 
in low frequencies of DCs in the TME, which favors tumor growth [47].  
 
Figure 4 The cancer immunity cycle. The initial stages of the cycle involve the recognition of the malignant 
cells by the immune cells (Elimination stage). Those cell migrate to the TME in response to a 
==PAGE:14==
[9] 
 
chemokine/cytokine gradient, which is sensed by the appropriate receptors displayed on the surface of the 
cells. Effector cells like NK cells, lymphocytes (CD4 and CD8 T cells) and phagocytes will attack the 
transformed cells in order to eliminate them. The mechanisms involved in the killing of the tumor cells 
include IFN-γ and the Prf/Gzm axis used by both NK and T cells. The professional phagocytes will then 
present tumor antigens via the MHC complexes, which will establish a T cell mediated immune response. As 
the cycle progresses the system will achieve an equilibrium in which some tumor cells, due to the mutational 
burden, will develop neoantigens and will downregulate the MHC-I molecules. These cells will have an 
evolutionary advantage over the rest of the tumor cells, and will hide from the immune system (Equilibrium 
stage). Even though the cells that downregulate or abolish the expression of MHC molecules are susceptible 
to NK cell mediated clearance, some tumor cells are able to escape the control of the immune system. Those 
parent cells will proliferate and generate more cancer cells. Furthermore, as the tumor progress, the TME 
acquires a more suppressive identity either directly from the cancer cells or indirectly by the recruitment 
(by means of chemoattractans) of immunosuppressive cells like Tregs, MDSC, N2 TANs and M2 TAMs. 
Those cells are known to express immune checkpoint inhibitors (ICI) like the programmed death ligand 1 
(PD-L1) and other factors that induce the suppression of the immune system. The formation of such a 
suppressive network hampers the antitumor immune responses and favors the survival of the tumor cells 
(Escape stage).  
Since NK cells recognize the transformed cells due to their stress-ligands and due to 
downregulation of MHC-I molecules, understanding their role in cancer immunosurveillance 
is crucial. The NK cell mediated immunosurveillance commences with the migration of NK 
cells to the tumor bed in response to chemokines secreted in the TME [48]. Once they arrive, 
the arsenal of activating and inhibitory receptors will scout for ligands expressed by the tumor 
cells [31, 48, 49]. As it is aforementioned, the “missing-self hypothesis” supports the idea of 
how NK cells can recognize the MHC-I negative tumors by the absence of inhibitory signal 
[31, 49, 50]. In this regard, NK cells have a shorter path to cancer elimination compared to 
CD8+ T cells, since the latter need presentation of cognate antigens on MHC-I molecules to 
elicit an antitumor response [27, 51]. However, not all NK cells are licensed and not all 
tumors downregulate the MHC-I molecules [31]. In this case, the “un-licensed” NK cells that 
cannot recognize MHC-I molecules via the inhibitory receptors, can eliminate those tumors 
[31]. Moreover, the profound proliferation of malignant cells is accompanied by the 
upregulation of the ligand retinoic acid early transcript 1 (RAE1), a ligand for activating 
NKG2D receptor of NK cells [52]. 
The TME, however, is a plastic space, subjected to constant changes. These changes 
(epigenetic alterations, expression differences, recruitment of suppressive cells, changes in the 
cytokine milieu) influence the antitumor responses, and in the plethora of cases, the result is 
an immunosuppressive TME, in which cancer cells can evade the immune system [24, 45, 53, 
54]. As it is the case with every other immune cell, NK cells are greatly affected by the TME. 
Cancer cells can produce various factors that modulate the functionality of NK cells, directly 
or indirectly. Indirectly, those factors may recruit T regulatory cells (Treg), neutrophils (Nφ) 
and myeloid-derived suppressor cells (MDSC), cells known to hamper NK cells [40, 50, 53, 
54]. Furthermore, those secreted factors can immediately affect NK cells by binding to 
specific receptors. As an example, the transforming-growth factor β (TGF-β), prostaglandin 
E2 (PGE-2), indoleamine 2,3-dioxygenase (IDO) and IL-10 constitute certain of the 
immunosuppressive factors that, after binding to their respective receptors can diminish NK 
cell cytotoxicity and hamper their functions (Figure 5) [53-55]. In human tumors, the TME 
cytokine/chemokine milieu changes in a way that attracts more CD56bright NK cells via 
upregulated expression of CXCL9, CXCL10, and prohibits the migration of cytotoxic 
CD56dim NK cells by reducing the expression of CXCL1, CXCL2 and CXCL8 [56]. Finally, a 
rather ingenuous, yet potentially problematic immune escaping mechanism devised by cancer 
cells includes the shedding of MHC-I molecules from their surface [49].  In certain myeloid 
malignancies, soluble ligands for activating and inhibitory receptors were shown to act as 
decoys favoring the survival of the tumor cells [57]. This shedding has been shown to be 
mediated by the proteases ADAM10 and ADAM17 [58]. This interplay between the TME 
and the NK cells, results in the downregulation of activating receptors (CD16, NKG2D) and 
subsequently the diminished cytotoxicity of the latter against malignant cells [50, 54, 59].  
==PAGE:15==
[10] 
 
 
Figure 5 NK cell immunosuppression induced by tumor associated immune cells. The TME is a highly 
plastic network in which cells are able to modulate effector functions of their neighboring cells. Specifically, 
immune cells with a more suppressive phenotype like Tregs, MDSC, TANs and TAMs as well as DCs  (a IL-
10 and TGF-β producing subset termed regulatory DCs) are known to hamper the effector functions of NK 
cells and T cells. From the NK cell point of view, TGF-β secreted by the aforementioned cells suppresses the 
NK cells. Other factors with suppressive properties towards the NK cells include IL-10, IDO, PGE-2 and 
ROS. As a result, NK cells display a less cytotoxic phenotype as seen by the downregulation of activating 
receptors and the upregulation of inhibitory receptors. Moreover, they seem to express less effector 
molecules like IFN-γ, TNF-α and GzmB. These alterations render them unable to recognize and lyse the 
tumor cell.  
The overall effect of the TME on the NK cells is mirrored on their phenotypic alterations. 
These observations have introduced the concept of NK cell plasticity, by which NK cells can 
swift to a different, even tumor-promoting phenotype in response to TME stimuli [60]. TGF-
β, both in soluble or membrane bound form, is shown to convert the murine NK cells into 
ILC1, which lack cytotoxic properties [61]. Additionally, TGF-β inhibits IFN-γ production by 
T-bet inhibition and downregulates the expression of NKG2D, hampering the tumor 
immunosurveillance [53, 61]. Surprisingly, Casu et al. demonstrated that IL-18, a stimulatory 
cytokine, can act synergistically with TGF-β, leading to the downregulation of NKp30 
activating receptor of NK cells [62]. Similarly, PGE-2 expressing tumors inhibit the NK-DC 
mediated antitumor axis, by affecting both NK and DC [46]. In fact, reports on human tumors 
demonstrate that the infiltrating NK cells are non-cytotoxic CD56brightPrflow , suggesting an 
immediate link between the TME and the phenotypic modifications of NK cells [60]. Krneta 
et al. showed, by using a breast tumor model, that NK cells in the TME acquire an immature 
phenotype (CD11b-CD27-CD49b-), with low expression of activating receptors like NKp30 
and NKG2D and higher expression of the inhibitory NKG2A receptor [63]. The subset of 
CD11b and CD27 double negative NK cells in hepatocellular carcinoma patients is associated 
with poor prognosis [60]. Finally, samples acquired from patients with lymph node 
metastases are characterized by NK cells with a higher expression of the co-inhibitory 
==PAGE:16==
[11] 
 
receptors TIM-3 and TIGIT and inadequate antitumor effect [60]. In lung adenocarcinoma 
and gastrointestinal tumor patients, NK cells from the blood steam and the TME, respectively,  
have upregulated TIM-3 and displayed an exhausted phenotype [64]. 
1.2.4 NK cells, Neutrophils and Cancer cell metastasis: a “Ménage à 
trois”   
1.2.4.1 Cancer Metastasis 
Metastasis is the process of tumor cell colonization of secondary tissues in the body [65]. The 
metastatic cascade involves various steps, beginning with the formation of blood vessels, 
following the dissemination of cancer cells from the primary tumor niche and their 
introduction to the bloodstream, ending with their attachment to a distant tissue (lung, liver 
etc.) and the formation of a new colony (Figure 6) [65-67].  The initial step, the 
vascularization of the tumor bed, commences with the secretion of pro-angiogenic factors 
from the cells of the TME (including cancer cells, immune cells and epithelial cells 
surrounding the tumor) [65-67]. The best characterized factor involved into this process is the 
vascular-endothelial growth factor (VEGF), which upon interacting with its respective 
receptors, induces the sprouting of new vessels [67]. Following the extravasation, certain 
cancer cells undergo a phenotypic switch known as epithelial to mesenchymal transition 
(EMT) [65]. This process endows the transformed cells with more aggressive and invasive 
properties enabling them to detach from the primary tumor (loss of anchoring to the 
extracellular matrix (ECM) by downregulating adhering molecules like E-cadherin) and enter 
the circulation as circulating tumor cells (CTC) [65, 68]. In order to escape the circulation, 
CTCs form protrusions called invadopodia, which are also endowed with proteases that 
degrade the ECM [69]. Certain immune cells support the tumor cells during this process, and 
facilitate their escape from the pre-metastatic niche. These cells include MDSC, neutrophils, 
macrophages, DCs, and Tregs [52, 70]. The main mechanisms aiding the immune-mediated 
metastatic cascade involve the production and secretion of both immunosuppressive and pro-
angiogenic factors like VEGF and angiopoietin 1, as well as factors that modulate and modify 
the ECM such as matrix metalloproteases 9 (MMP9) [52, 70]. Platelets are also involved in 
safeguarding the CTCs by physically shielding them [68].  Nonetheless, the disseminated 
tumor cells (DTCs) that are subjected to this molecular alterations leading to EMT face a 
stressful situation, that may activate a type of cellular death called anoikis, which is induced 
due to the loss of cell to cell adhesion [71, 72]. The surviving DTCs are rather vulnerable to 
the circulating immune cells during their course through the blood [52, 73]. In fact, NK cells, 
macrophages and Th1 cells play an important role in the clearance of CTC from the 
circulation [52, 74]. 
==PAGE:17==
[12] 
 
 
Figure 6 Tumor cell metastasis from the primary tumor site to distant tissues. Tumor cells have the capacity 
to migrate from the primary tumor site to other tissues throughout the body by entering the systemic 
circulation. The process of metastasis is a multi-step procedure starting from the degradation of the ECM. 
This involves the enzymatic digestion of the connective tissue by various factors like MMPs. The next step 
involves the disruption of the vasculature and the formation of new vessels in the tumor bed by the 
generation of angiogenic factors like the VEGF family and other growth factors. Concurrently, certain 
tumor cells undergo a reprogramming in order to acquire a more motile and invasive phenotype. This 
process is called EMT. Those cells that will survive this process will form the pre -metastatic niche, from 
where they will escape to the circulation. Through the blood stream they will travel to distant tissues 
(macrometastasis) and form new colonies.  
1.2.4.2 Implication of NK cells in metastasis 
The importance of NK cells in metastatic control has become clear, since data collected from 
patients with various cancers suggest a link between the increased numbers of NK cells in the 
circulation and the occurrence of metastatic lesions [73]. During EMT, transformed cells 
downregulate ligands that upon interaction with certain NK cell receptors, would have 
rendered the latter anergic [68]. In the light of this event, NK cells can be activated due to the 
lack of an inhibitory signal and eradicate the malignant cell through the GzmB/Prf axis, or by 
the formation of an immunological synapse involving death ligands like FasL [74].This 
discovery has led to the development of treatment strategies that include priming NK cells 
and adoptively transferring them to target CTCs and DTCs [74, 75]. Moreover, Krzywinska et 
al. demonstrated that NK cells hamper the angiogenic cascade by secreting a soluble form of 
VEGF receptor 1 (sVEGFR1), acting as a decoy to occupy VEGF secreted in the TME, thus 
procrastinating the vessel formation [75]. IL-18 was shown to upregulate FasL on liver 
resident ILC1, associating their role with protection against metastasis [10]. Finally, high 
infiltration of NK cells expressing NKp46 and IFN-γ is associated with good prognosis for 
prostate cancer, and are able to kill metastatic melanoma cells both in vivo and in vitro, 
further highlighting their importance in controlling metastases [73]. 
==PAGE:18==
[13] 
 
Despite the fact that NK cells are highly cytotoxic against malignant cells, there are reports 
suggesting that these cells may also have a helper effect during the metastatic process. This 
has risen by the observation of NK cells harboring angiogenic properties. These cells 
resemble the decidual NK cells (dNK), which during pregnancy secrete VEGF and placental 
growth factor (PIGF) [70, 76]. In humans these cells display a CD56superbrightCD16+ phenotype 
and can also secrete IL-10 [70]. In non-small cell lung cancer patients NK cells were seen to 
acquire such a decidual-like phenotype [76]. In an experimental model, Cerdeira et al. 
managed to swift the phenotype of NK cells towards dNK by TGF-β and in presence of 
hypoxia [77]. Interestingly, another population belonging to the innate lymphoid cells, the 
ILC3 was associated with the promotion of lymphagiogenesis [70]. Recently, in human 
hypoxic breast tumors, systemic factors were shown to increase the infiltration of CD11b+ 
cells while impeding the cytotoxic effect of NK cells [78]. Lo et al. reported that there is an 
“co-evolutionary” relationship between NK cells and metastatic cells [79]. In fact, the authors 
found out that NK cells are more effective in killing single cells metastatic cells, while 
leaving the clustered disseminated cells unharmed. This is due to phenotypic differences like 
loss of anchorage in single cells compared to clusters, and the upregulation or downregulation 
of NK activating ligands [79]. Zhao et al. showed that erythrocytes coated with a CXCL10 
nanoparticle could induce the accumulation of effector cells like CD8 T cells and NK cells in 
the metastasized lungs [80]. The authors also observed increased infiltration of DCs in the 
lungs, possibly invigorating the NK-DC axis, which is suggested to favor the antigen 
presentation by DCs to lymphocytes [80].  
1.2.4.3 The role of Neutrophils in cancer & metastasis 
Neutrophils (also known as polymorphonuclear neutrophils (PMN)) constitute important 
players of the immune system, and are the first cells to respond in the event of inflammation 
in response to CXCL1 (in mice) and CXCL5. They develop in the BM from myeloblasts in 
response to granulocyte colony-stimulating factor (G-CSF) [81]. IL-6, IL-3 and GM-CSF to 
induce the production of granulocytes like Nφ, a process known as granulopoiesis [82]. 
Human neutrophils express CD66b, while the murine ones have the lymphocyte antigen 6G 
(Ly6G) [83]. However, as far as the nomenclature of neutrophils is difficult considering they 
share functional and phenotypical traits with PMN-MDSC [78, 84]. Their exit from the BM is 
controlled by chemokine receptors (like CXCR2 and CXCR4), while abundant production of 
CXCL12 by the stromal cells keep the Nφ in the BM [83]. PMN exist in two phenotypic 
states: the pro-inflammatory termed N1 and the pro-tumorigenic N2 [52]. The N1 phenotype 
is maintained in response to type I interferons, while the N2 is induced by TGF-β [85, 86]. 
Anti-tumorigenic Nφ are characterized by TNF-α, CD54, nitric oxide produced by the 
enzymatic activity of inducible nitric oxide synthase (iNOS) and low arginase 1 (Arg-1) 
levels and the generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS), which mediate cancer cell killing 
[86-88]. In contrast, N2 cells are known to express high levels of Arg-1, programmed-death 
ligand 1 (PD-L1), ROS and pro-angiogenic factors like MMP9 [85, 87, 89]. Ohms et al. 
demonstrated that it is possible to swift between the N1 and N2 phenotypes in vitro, by using 
the necessary cytokine cocktail [85]. Moreover, Vokalova et al. demonstrated that galectin-
13, a protein expressed in the placenta of primates, induces a N2-like phenotype displaying 
high levels of PD-L1, TNF-α, ROS and neutrophil extracellular traps (NETs) [89]. In any 
case, both N1 and N2 Nφ contain granules with various proteins having immune functions 
(myeloperoxidase (MPO), cathepsin G, S100-A8/9, TNF, IL-1, TGF-β, activing A), are 
associated 
with 
the 
cytoskeleton 
(β-actin) 
as 
well 
as 
“house-keeping” 
proteins 
(glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate dehydrogenase (GAPDH)) [90]. In the pro-inflammatory 
setting, Ethuin et al. reported that neutrophils were able to secrete IFN-γ, which was stored in 
their granules, upon stimulation with IL-12 [91]. Tumors can systemically control the fate of 
the neutrophils as in mouse models G-CSF influenced the production of immunosuppressive 
neutrophils [47].  
The importance of understanding the role of neutrophils in cancer arises from the fact that in 
many tumors and especially in breast cancer patients, high numbers of neutrophils compared 
==PAGE:19==
[14] 
 
to lymphocytes (CD4+ and CD8+ T cells) is associated with poor prognosis [88]. Nφ migrate 
to the TME in response to chemokines like IL-8 and IL-17, with γδ T cells being the major 
sources of the latter [87, 88]. Epithelial cells in the tumor stroma are capable of recruiting Nφ 
via the secretion of high-mobility group box 1 (HMGB1), which interacts with the damage-
associated molecular sensing receptor RAGE expressed by Nφ[88]. Once they arrive in the 
tumor bed, the tumor-associated neutrophils (TAN) exert their pro-tumorigenic and 
immunosuppressive properties. N2 TAN induce immunosuppression by means of ROS 
(induces oxidation of the CD3ζ chain of T cells hampering its function) and Arg-1 (which 
depletes the L-arginine, an essential amino acid for T cell survival and function) [81, 92]. 
Moreover, in a humanized model of hepatocellular carcinoma, it was demonstrated that TAN 
can indirectly support the establishment of an immunosuppressive TME by recruiting 
macrophages and Tregs [93]. Surprisingly, in early stages of lung cancer, and also in 
homeostatic conditions in the lymph nodes, researchers observed a non-canonical Nφ 
population expressing MHC class II molecules, and were able to activate T cells [83, 93]. 
Moreover, these antigen presenting cell (APC)-like Nφ secreted pro-inflammatory cytokines 
like IFN-γ, IL-12 and TNF [93]. Just like NK cells, Nφ express the Fcγ receptor (CD16) that 
enables them to engage with antibody opsonized tumor cells and initiate the ADCC [94]. 
Matlung et al. showed that Nφ can kill opsonized solid tumors via ADCC-mediated disruption 
of the plasma membrane, a process termed trogocytosis [94]. Additionally, blockade of TGF-
β in the TME resulted in accumulation of murine CD11b+Ly6G+ Nφ with cytotoxic effect 
against tumor cells [95]. Finally, Nφ can activate macrophages to produce more IL-12 and, 
subsequently prime unconventional T cells (CD4-CD8-T cell receptor (TCR)αβ+, UTCαβ) to 
produce IFN-γ [83]. 
In addition to their immunosuppressive potential, TANs can also facilitate tumor growth and 
initiation of metastasis [96]. Their pro-tumorigenic effect is mediated by the secretion of 
angiogenic factors (VEGF, fibroblast growth factor (FGF), hepatocyte growth factor (HGF)) 
and ECM remodeling enzymes (MMP8, MMP9, neutrophil elastase (NE), cathepsin G) [86, 
88, 90]. IL-1β is also a cytokine produced by TAN that primes the endothelial cells, disrupts 
the endothelial barrier and facilitates the trans-endothelial migration of DTCs [86]. MMPs 
facilitate metastasis by digesting the ECM and releasing the angiogenic factors (e.g. VEGF) 
that are retained in a bound and inactive form [69]. A protective mechanism that is exploited 
during cancer progression is the formation of NETs, a process called NETosis [81]. Those 
NTEs are comprised by decondensated chromatin that is released by the Nφ, as an act of 
“suicide mission” against bacterial infection [81]. Hsu et al. discovered that immature low 
density Nφ facilitate liver metastasis via the production of FGF and NETosis [97]. NETs act 
as a platform for the aggregation of CTCs, forming this way a pre-metastatic niche [81, 86]. 
Other proteins trapped in the NETs (like HMGB1, NE and MMP9) promote the proliferation 
of the NET anchored CTCs [83]. TAN derived cytokines IL-1β, IL-6 and IL-17α were 
mentioned to initiate EMT in cancer cells [88].  Engblom et al. found a pro-tumorigenic Nφ 
population expressing sialic acid-binding immunoglobulin-like lectin F (Siglec-F) also known 
as CD170, that produced high levels of ROS [98]. In fact, they showed that osteoblasts were 
supplying the lung tumors with these Siglec-Fhigh Nφ [98]. In melanoma patients, HMGB1 
facilitates metastasis formation by activating toll-like receptor 4 (TLR4) on Nφ [87]. Finally, 
Liang et al. reported that, in a 4T1 metastatic model of breast cancer, the transferrin secreted 
by TAN acted as a mitogenic agent favoring lung metastasis [99]. Additionally, GM-CSF 
derived from 4T1 cells functioned as a signal for de novo transferrin synthesis by the 
neutrophils [99]. Spiegel et al. also showed that G-CSF excreted from 4T1 cells induces the 
expansion of Nφ, and in turn the latter facilitate the formation of metastatic lesions in the 
lungs [100]. Another example of how TME can exploit a normally protective mechanism of 
neutrophils in oncostatin-M. Normally, oncostatin-M expressing TANs exert an anti-
proliferative effect on breast tumor cells. Alas N2 TANs harboring oncostatin-M were able to 
stimulate breast cancer cell line metastasis [101].  Finally, even though not linked with 
metastasis, it is important to mention that Ly6G+ iNOS expressing cells were shown to be 
responsible for the reoccurrence of tumor in glioblastoma patients [102]. Jeon et al. found that 
==PAGE:20==
[15] 
 
those inflammatory TANs were able to induce dedifferentiation of glioblastoma cells towards 
glioblastoma stem cells, a process that was mediated by iNOS and nitric oxide production 
[102]. Depletion of the Ly6G inflammatory neutrophils prolonged the survival of irradiated 
mice [102].

1.2.4.4 The NK/Neutrophil axis in metastasis 
Despite the fact that both NK cells and Nφ have anti-tumorigenic activities, their exact 
relationship regarding tumor and metastasis control remains unclear. NK cells are known to 
kill CTCs in the blood, while neutrophils tend to shield them from immune clearance, even 
though Nφ may also exert anti-tumorigenic functions in the primary tumor site (Figure 7) [74, 
86, 90, 100].  It has been shown that neutrophils can suppress the cytotoxic properties of NK 
cells, and most notably by means of ROS [103]. However, in the pre-metastatic niche, Nφ 
educated by cancer cells exhibit a tumor cytotoxic function also mediated by ROS [104]. In 
addition, ROS produced by granulocytes were able to downregulate the NKp46 activating 
receptor of NK cells, resulting in diminished tumor killing [103]. So how do those complex 
relations affect the metastatic cascade? Li et al. demonstrated that Nφ could inhibit lung 
resident NK cell function (low IFN-γ and CD107a expression) in a 4T1 model of metastasis 
[92]. Spiegel et al. also showed that Nφ act by fostering DTCs and enabling their metastasis 
in the lungs, and that this process was facilitated by the G-CSF secreted by the 4T1 cells 
[100]. NK cell depletion abolished the clearance of CTCs [100]. In accordance, Li et al. found 
that G-CSF mediated neutrophilia augmented the ROS production and ergo the 
immunosuppression of NK cells in the metastatic lungs [92]. Moreover, by systemically 
depleting NK cells, neutrophils exerted a tumoricidal effect, while in their presence they 
displayed a net pro-tumorigenic effect by suppression the latter [92]. Co-culture experiments 
showed that TAN hamper the activation of NK cells, as seen due to the downregulation of 
CD69, an early activation marker expressed by NK cells [105]. Arg-1 mediated depletion of 
arginine was also held responsible for the diminished NK cell cytotoxicity [103]. Thus, the 
functional interplay between neutrophils and NK cells in the TME, the pre-metastatic niche 
and the circulation is responsible for the metastatic dissemination and the progression of the 
tumor growth. These pieces of evidence also highlight the complexity of the relationship 
between the host immune status (presence or absence of certain immune cells) and the tumor 
promotion [92].  
==PAGE:21==
[16] 
 
 
Figure 7 Metastatic control by NK cells and Neutrophils in the TME and the circulation. NK cells, 
neutrophils and cancer cells appear to have a rather complicated relationship during the metastatic cascade. 
Originally, NK cells and neutrophils are able to kill tumor cells. However, in later stages neutrophils act as a 
“royal guard” protecting the tumor cells from NK cell clearance. This is mediated either by direct coverage 
of the tumor cells by neutrophils, hiding them from the NK cells, or indirectly by the production of ROS. 
Although ROS is also toxic to cancer cells, a balance is achieved that hampers the antitumor NK cell 
function and leads to the tumor escape. In the absence of such suppression by neutrophils, NK cells in the 
circulation can recognize the tumor cells and kill them via the mechanisms explained in previous chapters of 
this document.  
1.2.5 NK cell based Treatment Options for Cancer 
It is understandable that due to their uniqueness; NK cells have attracted a lot of attention 
regarding their application in cancer treatment over the past years. In fact many NK cell based 
treatment options have been devised through the years (Table 3). The very first trials to use 
the host’s NK cells as a form of immunotherapy involved the blocking of inhibitory receptors 
or inhibitory immune checkpoints expressed by the NK cells and administrating activating 
cytokines or adjuvants that would result in the activation of the patient’s immune system 
[106]. In recent years, another form of NK cell therapy arose in the form of adoptive cell 
transfer [106]. In order for the cell infusion to be efficient, a great number of NK cells are 
necessary. There are many different sources for NK cell expansion in vitro, for instance 
peripheral blood mononuclear cells can be isolated from both patients (autologous) or from 
healthy donors (allogenic), or HSCs can be isolated from cord blood and expanded to NK 
cells [106, 107]. Another option for obtaining a sufficient number of NK cells for adoptive 
transfer is through the dedifferentiation of induced pluripotent stem cells (iPSC) to HSC and 
then to NK cells [107, 108]. Moreover, with the development of gene editing tools, many 
groups have developed genetically engineered NK cells that express a receptor that 
recognizes a specific tumor antigen [108]. These chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) NK cells 
have been used to treat hematologic malignancies by generating CAR-NK cells targeting 
CD19 [109]. Apart from those strategies, other options have been deployed to harness the NK 
cell antitumor potential that include pre-conditioning of NK cells with cytokines like IL-2, IL-
12, IL-15 and IL-18, as well as the development of bi- or tri-specific molecules baring 
antibody domains that ligate different targets (e.g. an activating receptor on the surface of the 
NK cells and a tumor antigen) [108, 109]. Those molecules are termed bi-specific killer 
==PAGE:22==
[17] 
 
engagers (BiKES) and tri-specific killer engagers (TriKES) [109]. Finally, immortalized NK 
cell lines have been developed that exert anti-tumorigenic effect, with the NK-92 being 
approved for clinical trials [106]. In a phase 2 trial (NCT01181258), Bachanova et al. showed 
that effectiveness of adoptively transferred NK cells is linked to the immunosuppressive state 
(presence of Tregs and MDSC) of the recipient [110]. In fact, high expression of TIGIT was 
associated with failure to respond, while elevated IL-15 followed the NK cell transfer was 
linked to therapeutic efficacy [110].  
Table 3 NK cell based treatment options for solid and hematological malignancies. REFs 
Regiments

Malignancy

1.2.5.1 IL-12 based NK cell strategies for cancer immunotherapy 
Priming NK cells with cytokines prior to adoptive cell transfer is a rather attractive option that 
has been explored quite a lot in cancer immunotherapy. The most well established cytokines 
to boost NK cell activity include type I interferons and interleukins like IL-2,IL-12,IL-15 and 
IL-18 [111]. However, in this chapter focuses on the IL-12 mediated NK cell preconditioning.  
IL-12 is a known inducer of IFN-γ by both NK cells and T cells (Th1) by activating the Janus 
Kinase (JAK) and signal transducer and activator of transcription (STAT) or mitogen-
activated protein kinase (MAPK) signaling pathways [112]. In the context of tumor, IL-12 
was shown to enhance the anti-tumor immunity (mediated by T cells and NK cells), while 
suppressing the immunosuppressive Tregs [112]. Moreover, intravenous (i.v.), intraperitoneal 
(i.p.), intratumoral (i.t.) as well as subcutaneous (s.c.) IL-12 administrations were shown to 
have a protective effect by delaying the tumor growth in many experimental murine cancer 
models [113]. Ohs et al. also demonstrated that exogenous IL-12 can activate an emergency 
NK cell generation program in vivo from the BM, leading to the production of 
CD122+CD49a+ NK cells [114]. Thus IL-12 has a dual function: that of activating NK cells 
and promoting the generation of unconventional, yet functional NK cells. Currently, multiple 
clinical trials investigating the therapeutic application of IL-12 against tumors are ongoing 
(Table 4).  
Zhuang et al. showed that IL-12/IL-15/IL-18 primed NK cells were able to eliminate 
hepatocellular carcinoma cells in vitro, while in an in vivo model, preconditioned NK cells 
migrated and protected the liver from spontaneous tumor formation [115]. Uppendahl et al. 
also observed that cytokine pre-activated NK cells had a tumoricidal effect against ovarian 
cancer, with the NK cells being more proliferative and activated based on the CD25 
expression [116]. Moreover, Tietje at al. developed an IL-12/MHC class I polypeptide-related 
sequence A (MICA) fusion protein, as a form of NK cell activation and anti-neoplastic 
mediator [117]. As MICA is a ligand for the NKG2D activating receptor, they authors 
hypothesized that a fusion protein with IL-12 and an activating ligand, could induce a potent 
anti-tumor NK cell response. Indeed, in their model. NK92 cells showed increased IFN-γ 
production and tumoricidal effect after exposure to this IL-12/MICA construct [117]. Ohs et 
al. demonstrated that IL-12 administration in combination with immune checkpoint inhibition 
==PAGE:23==
[18] 
 
accounted for tumor control by NK cells and exerted an antimetastatic effect as seen by the 
reduction to pulmonary metastases in a preclinical model [118]. The antimetastatic effect of 
IL-12 is connected to the IFN-γ production. IFN-γ induced the production of CXCL10, a 
potent attractant of NK cells and T cells, with the newly migrated NK cells playing an 
important role in angiogenesis inhibition and tumor growth inhibition [111]. All the above 
evidence highlights the significant role of NK cells in modulating tumor progression both in 
vitro and in vivo, and crowns IL-12 as a key cytokine mediating anti-tumor responses.

2. Aim of the study 
The aim of this study is to evaluate the efficacy of different IL-12 based immunotherapy 
formulations, either as monotherapies or combinational therapy, with a focus on the role of 
NK cells in the metastatic 4T1 cancer model. The hypothesis is that IL-12 mediated activation 
of NK cells will eliminate CTCs in the circulation, thus decreasing the presence of metastatic 
lesions in the lungs.  
In order to evaluate the preclinical value of these studies, different flow cytometry panels are 
generated to investigate the immune cell phenotypes in the TME, as well as other secondary 
organs. Specifically, the panels will contain markers to distinguish the NK cells among 
lymphocytes and also myeloid cells present in the TME, the spleen and the lungs. Moreover, 
since the 4T1 model is a metastatic one, we wish to investigate the metastasis formation in the 
lungs and the effect of the different treatments on cancer dissemination. Additionally, 
considering the role of NK cells in controlling metastasis formation and their interplay with 
the neutrophils, we wish to delineate the relationships between NK cells and neutrophils, and 
to clarify their role in the disease progression, during our efficacy studies. Finally, to clarify 
the role of those aforementioned cell populations in response to the different treatments, we 
will conduct some depletion experiments (depletion of neutrophils), so as to understand the 
roles and possible mechanisms behind their action in this model. 
Evaluating the effect of IL-12 regimens on this metastatic cancer model will enable our group 
to device additional approaches in different cancer models. Last but not least, acquiring more 
insights on the roles NK cells and neutrophils in response to treatments may pave the way for 
more efficacious and targeted therapies.  
3. Materials & Methods 
3.1 Cancer Cell Line 
The 4T1 mammary carcinoma cell line was purchased from the American Type Culture 
Collection (ATCC® CRL-2539™), and was used as the syngeneic cancer model for this 
thesis project.  The cell line was tested for pathogens upon arrival and before being used in 
our experimental set-ups. Cells were stored in a cryopreservation tank filled with liquid 
nitrogen in cryo-tubes in the presence of 90% Fetal Bovine Serum (FBS, #1050064, Thermo 
Fisher Scientific) and 10% Dimethyl Sulfoxide (#D2650, Sigma-Aldrich). Before the in vivo 
studies, 4T1 cells were thawed and cultured for 7 days in a TPP Tissue Culture Flask 
(#90076, Buch & Holm) with RPIMI-1640 Medium, Glutamax™ Supplement at 37°C 
(#61870, Thermo Fisher Scientific), supplemented with 10 % FBS and 1 % Penicillin-
Streptomycin (10,000 U/mL) (#15140, Thermo Fisher Scientific). Cells were split by rinsing 
with Dulbecco’s phosphate buffer saline without Ca2+ and without Mg2+ (PBS) (#ALB-
L0615, Biowest) to remove extra medium. Following this, Trypsin-EDTA 0.25 % was added 
(#T4049, Sigma-Aldrich) and the flask was kept in an incubator at 37°C for 5 min to detach 
adherent cells followed by the addition of RPMI-1640 to stop the effect of trypsin. 
Approximately 70% of the cells were collected and re-cultured to a new flask supplemented 
with the aforementioned growth medium. On the day of the inoculation, cells were harvested 
by adding Trypsin-EDTA 0.25 %, and then washed by the addition of Hanks’ Balanced Salt 
Solution (#14025092, Thermo Fisher Scientific) and centrifuged at 300xG for 5 min. Finally, 
cells were re-suspended in HBSS to a final concentration of 5x104.  
==PAGE:25==
[20] 
 
3.2 Animal Tumor Model & In Vivo Studies 
6-week old female BALB/cJrJ mice were purchased from Janvier Labs (Le Genest, France), 
and housed in the Department of Experimental Medicine, University of Copenhagen, and the 
Animal Facilities at the Technical University of Denmark. Mice were acclimatized for at least 
a week before the initiation to the study had access to water and chow ad libitum throughout 
the project. All animal experiments were conducted in accordance to the Danish Animal 
Experiments Inspectorate, under the directive 2010/63/EU of the European Parliament on the 
protection of animal used in scientific set-ups.  
Mice were injected with 5x104 4T1 cells subcutaneously (s.c.) in the right flank in an 
injection volume of 100ul/mouse. Tumors were allowed to grow for at least a week before 
randomization and then mice were separated into treatment groups based on the tumor 
volume. Treatments were initiated the following day after randomization. Tumor size was 
measured every 2 days using a caliber and the volume was calculated using the following 
formula: 
𝑇𝑢𝑚𝑜𝑟 𝑉𝑜𝑙𝑢𝑚𝑒 (𝑚𝑚3) = 𝐿𝑒𝑛𝑔ℎ𝑡 (𝑚𝑚)𝑥 (𝑊𝑖𝑑𝑡ℎ (𝑚𝑚))
2𝑥 0,5 
For the efficacy studies, mice were monitored every 2 days (tumor and weight measurement). 
Mice were euthanized when tumors reaches and/or surpassed the primary endpoint of a tumor 
volume 1000mm3. The weight was monitored for toxicity purposes following each treatment. 
Mice were euthanized if they exhibited a weight loss more than 20% after tumor challenge or 
more than 15% between the consecutive measurements, or if wounds were detected upon the 
site of injection on the primary tumor.  
3.3 Ex Vivo Flow Cytometry   
At the end of each study, spleens, tumors and lungs were harvested for flow cytometry. The 
organs were stored in MACS tissue storage solution on ice. In order to obtain single cell 
suspensions, the organs were passed through a 70μm strainer and washed with PBS. Tumors 
and lungs were enzymatically digested in tumor tissue dissociation kit (Miltenyi Biotech) for 
an hour in 37°C in a shacking water bath. To eliminate red blood cells, spleen pellets were re-
suspended in 1ml VersaLyse lysing solution (Beckman Coulter) and incubated for 10 min at 
room temperature. Subsequently, cells were washed in fluorescence-activated cell sorting 
buffer (FACS buffer), consisting of PBS, 0.5 % bovine serum albumin (#A7906, Sigma-
Aldrich), 0.1% sodium azide (#S2002, Sigma-Aldrich), and 2 mM Ethylenediaminetetraacetic 
acid (EDTA) (#27285, Sigma-Aldrich), and then transferred to a U-bottom 96-well plate. 
After transfer to the plate, the pellets were re-suspended in blocking solution containing rat 
anti-mouse CD16/CD32 (FcγRIII/ FcγRII, #553142, BD Biosciences) (0.05 mg/mL) in FACS 
buffer and incubated for at least 5 min in ice to prevent unspecific binding of antibodies. The 
fluorophore-conjugated monoclonal antibodies CD45, CD11b, CD49b, Siglec-F, Ly-6G, 
F4/80, CD115, CD3ε, CD8, TIM-3, TIGIT, Arginase-1, iNOS, PD-L1, IFN-γ, GzmB, CD25, 
CXCR3, NKp46, I-E/I-A (MHC-II), AH-1 antigen specific Dextramer and Fixable Viability 
Dye (VD) eFlour™ 780 (#65-0865-18, Thermo Fisher Scientific). The surface staining was 
done in FACS buffer, while the intracellular staining was performed in permeabilization 
buffer (PBS with 0.5% bovine serum albumin and 0.1% saponin). Brilliant Stain Buffer Plus 
(BD Biosciences) was included in the antibody master mix to enhance the staining. For a full 
overview of the fluorophore-conjugated antibodies see Supplementary Table 1. 
Sample acquisition was performed on a BD LSRFortessa X20 flow cytometer. For the 
compensation were used single-stained ArC™ Amine Reactive Compensation Beads 
(ThermoFischer Scientific), AbC™ Total Antibody Compensation Beads (ThermoFischer 
Scientific) or BD™ CompBeads Compensation Particles Anti-Rat/Hamster Ig,κ (BD 
Biosciences). The datasets were analyzed in FlowJo V.10.6.2 software.  
==PAGE:26==
[21] 
 
 3.4 In Vitro Colonogenic Assay 
Mice were euthanized by cervical dislocation, and the lungs were harvested in 5ml Eppendorf 
tubes containing sterile HBSS on ice. The lungs were then transferred onto petri dishes and 
minced into small pieces. Afterwards, the samples were re-suspended in murine tissue 
dissociation enzyme mix for 1 hour in 37°C in a shacking water bath. Following the digestion 
step, the lungs were rinsed with HBSS and then passed through a 70μm strainer. The cells 
were centrifuges at 400g for 5min. The pellets were then re-suspended in RPMI culture 
medium supplemented with 6-Thioguanine (6-TG Sigmal-Aldrich). After achieving the 
desired cell concentration, cells were plated in 6 well plates for 10 days. After the passage of 
this time, cells were rinsed with MiliQ water, fixated in methanol and stained with methylene 
blue solution 0,05% wt. in H20 (Sigma-Aldrich). Visible blue metastatic colonies were 
manually counted.  
3.5 Treatments for the In Vivo Studies 
Murine IL-12 (hence forward will be referred to as native IL-12), mouse serum albumin 
bound IL-12 (MSA IL-12), doxorubicin and an IL-12 bearing TriKE (hence forth mentioned 
as TrIL-12) were kindly provided through collaboration with Repertoire Immune Medicines. 
Due to intellectual property rights, it is not possible to disclose the exact details of the TrIL-
12 regimen.  
A day after mice were inoculated with the 4T1 cancer cell, the different groups received the 
aforementioned regimens via intravenous (i.v.) or subcutaneous (s.c.) injections to a 
concentration of 10ug per mouse. Doxorubicin was administered twice for only 1 week and 
the IL-12 formulations were given once a week until the conclusion of the studies.  
3.6 Neutrophil Depletion in vivo 
To deplete neutrophils anti-Ly6G (clone 1A8) was purchased from Bio X Cell. Mice were 
originally injected with 400ug i.p. and a supplementary dose of 100ug i.p. was given three 
times a week before treatment.  
3.7 Statistical Analysis 
All the statistical analyses were performed in Prism 6 for Windows (GraphPad Software, 
USA). Comparison between the groups was performed using the appropriate statistical test 
depending on whether or not the datasets were following the Gaussian distribution. For the 
survival analysis, Kaplan-Meiyer graphs were generated and compared using a Gehan-
Breslow-Wilcoxon test. The values were presented as mean ± SEM, unless stated otherwise.     
4. Results 
This thesis project investigates the potential of IL-12 based immunotherapies in the metastatic 
4T1 cancer model, but from the angle of NK cells and neutrophils.  
4.1 TrIL-12 & Doxorubicin: towards a synergistic effect? 
In this first efficacy study the objective was to investigate whether doxorubicin (anthracycline 
antibiotic) would have an additive effect on the TrIL-12 treatment. Doxorubicin was chosen 
since it is considered the standard of care in many chemotherapies, and has the reputation of 
being an effective drug against breast cancer [119]. Mice were inoculated with 5 × 105 cells 
subcutaneously (day 0), and after a week they were randomized based on tumor size and 
introduced to the study. Doxorubicin was administered directly after the initiation (day 7). 
TrIL-12 administration started the following week. Figure 8A shows the experimental setup 
for this study.  
Figure 8B depicts the mean tumor growth among the different treatment groups. Overall, 
there groups receiving the immunotherapy showed a slower tumor growth rate, compared to 
the HBSS treated group. However, no profound synergistic effect was observed in the 
==PAGE:27==
[22] 
 
combined treatment group. Figure 8C displays the mean body weight change in response to 
the treatments, in order to assess the toxicity. No significant toxicity was observed. Tumors 
on the day of the takedown had a uniform weight among the treatment groups, as seen in 
Figure 8D. Figures 8E to H show the individual tumor growth of each mouse in this study. 
In all groups tumor growth was continuous, albeit to a slower rate in the TrIL-12 receiving 
groups. Two mice in the doxorubicin group (Figure 8F) completely abolished the tumor by 
the day of the takedown, and they were excluded from any subsequent analysis.

Figure 8 Efficacy of doxorubicin and/or TrIL-12 as monotherapies or combined in mice with 4T1 tumors. 
A: experimental setup throughout the efficacy study. B: mean tumor growth curves (𝒎𝒎𝟑 ) of HBSS 
treated (blue) mice and those that received doxorubicin (red), TrIL-12 (light green) and doxorubicin with 
TrIL-12 (purple). C: relative body weight change to baseline of all the four groups. D: tumor weight (mg). 
E: individual tumor growth curves of the HBSS treated group. F: individual tumor growth curves of 
doxorubicin treated mice. G: individual tumor growth curves of the TrIL-12 group. H: individual tumor 
growth curves of the doxorubicin and TrIL-12 treated group. The error bars represent mean + SEM.  
 
4.1.1 Immune cell landscape of the TME in response to treatments. 
On the day of the takedown, tumors, spleens and lungs were harvested for flow cytometry 
analysis. For complete phenotyping of the immune cells refer to Supplementary Figures 1,2 
and 3. 
4.1.1.1 NK cells in the TME 
Frequency of total NK cells (CD49b+CD3-) was overall low in the TME as shown in Figure 
9A, with a slight increase in the doxorubicin treated group (3.3±0.57, 4.56±1.5, 2.56±0.61 & 
1.73±0.19, respectively in each group). CD11b was used to gate the three distinct NK cell 
subset: CD11b-, CD11b+(int) and CD11bhigh. The breast TME and in general tumor tissues are 
known to exclude NK cells and alter their phenotype towards a more immature/inactive state 
==PAGE:28==
[23] 
 
(CD11b-) [63, 120]. Figure 9B shows the percentage of CD11b- NK cells in the tumor. 
Indeed, in the HBSS treated group as well as in the doxorubicin group, their frequencies were 
higher compared to the TrIL-12 groups (46.25±7.67, 55.23±8.44, 22.19±3.14 & 29.25±3.1, 
respectively). The difference between the doxorubicin and TrIL-12 was significant (p-
value=0,0035), suggesting that TrIL-12 could promote the maturation and activation of 
CD11b- NK cells. This is consistent with the increased frequencies of CD11b+ (Figure 9C) 
(23.45±3.5, 17.03±4.4, 30.51±3.4 & 27.6±1.45, respectively) and CD11bhigh (Figure 9D) NK 
cells (28.85±5, 37.35±10.85, 47.05±5.49 & 42.64±3.18, respectively).  
As a functional signature, TIGIT was used to determine any signs of exhaustion on NK cells. 
TIGIT is an inhibitory receptor known to induce functional anergy on tumor-infiltrating NK 
cells [120, 121]. Figure 9E and 9F show that TIGIT+CD11b+ and TIGIT+CD11bhigh NK cells, 
respectively, were mostly present in HBSS (0.25±0.12 and 3.83±0.96, respectively) and 
doxorubicin (0.23±0.15 and 2.55±0.85, respectively) treated mice compared to TrIL-12 
(0.031±0.02 and 2.04±0.38, respectively)  and the combined groups  (0.1±0.05 and 0.86±0.12, 
respectively). The analysis showed that the difference between the HBSS and the combination 
group was significant (p-value=0,0065). Overall there were not many TIGIT+ NK cells in the 
TME, while no CD11b-TIGIT+ cells were detected.  
In order to assess if any of the treatment options had any effect on the expression of TIGIT in 
the TME, mean fluorescence intensity (MFI) was calculated. Figure 10 demonstrates the 
difference in the expression of TIGIT in the four groups. A decreasing trend in TIGIT 
expression was observed following the different treatments, with the lowest expression in the 
TrIL-12 monotherapy group.

combined groups (1.618e+006±775290, 480032±170391 and 662091±90322, respectively).

(p-value=0,0127).

4.1.1.2 Neutrophils in the TME 
Neutrophils are known to be deployed during carcinogenesis, and they are known to support 
tumor progression by various mechanisms. In the 4T1 model, neutrophils promote tumor 
growth, and hamper the anti-tumor effect of immune cells like NK cells [122]. A specific 
neutrophil population was described in the literature having pro-tumorigenic properties [96, 
98]. This population of interest was Siglec-Fhigh and was also identified in our studies 
(Supplementary Figure).  
Figure 12 demonstrates the effect of the treatments on the neutrophil populations. Generally, 
there is trend following the treatment administrations. In Figure 12A a reduction in the 
percentage of total neutrophils is noted, with doxorubicin having the highest decrease 
(19.07±9.95) compared to the other groups (HBSS, TrIL-12 and combination, 35.00±10.08, 
28.92±13.30 and 29.53±18.38, respectively).

The same depleting effect was observed in the Siglec-Fhigh population, with the TrIL-12 
having the highest reductive effect (4.19±2.51) compared to the other groups (HBSS 
7.06±3.6, Doxorubicin 4.41±2.3 and Doxorubicin+TrIL-12 4.94±2.19) (Figure 12B). Siglec-

particular changes in their frequencies (91.40±4.92, 91.47±3.21, 95.08±2.21 & 94.48±2.54, 
respectively).  
To get a clearer picture of the TME composition regarding the neutrophil populations, cells 
per gram of tumor were calculated to indicate neutrophil infiltration (Figure 13).

Doxorubicin+TrIL-12 (n=8, purple). Kruskal-Wallis test and Dunn’s multiple comparison test were used for 
this analysis in an a=0,05. The error bars represent the standard deviation (±SD).   
Decrease of total neutrophils in the TME was observed in response to treatment (Figure 
13A). However, a strong reduction was observed in the Siglec-Fhigh subpopulation (Figure 
13B). Specifically, TrIL-12 (223800±209466) treatment reduced the number of these cells 
significantly (p-value=0,007) compared to the HBSS (874707±561212) and doxorubicin 
(932958±698190). Combination of doxorubicin and TrIL-12 had a decreasing effect 
(438780±218244) but not of significant importance.    
==PAGE:31==
[26] 
 
As we were assessing the TME, we ran a t-distributed stochastic neighbor embedding (tSNE) 
analysis to acquire an unbiased picture of the immune cell composition. Doing so, we came 
upon a rather interesting cell population (Supplementary Figure 17), which was identified as 
neutrophils. This population was stained positive for the AH-1 antigen specific dextramer. 
Briefly, antigen specific dextramers are polymers bearing MHC-antigen complexes 
conjugated with fluorophores [123]. To our speculation, this neutrophil subset may be a T cell 
receptor (TCR) expressing population that encountered and recognised the AH-1 antigen. A 
TCR expressing neutrophil population has previously been described in humans and mice 
[124, 125] supporting this hypothesis. As presented in Figure 14A, there is an increase in this 
cell sub-population following treatment, with TrIL-12 having the greatest increase 
(9.17±6.02) in comparison with the other groups (HBSS: 3.72±3.26, Doxorubicin: 1.96±1.74 
and Doxorubicin+TrIL-12: 7.03±10.40). Regarding the TME infiltration (Figure 14B), there 
was also an increasing trend, but with the combinational group having the greatest number of 
those cells (1,996e+009±3,475e+009) compared to the other three groups (HBSS: 
1.137e+009±1.417e+009, 
Doxorubicin: 
5.507e+008±4.935e+008 
and 
TrIL-12: 
7.822e+008±6.927e+008).

4.1.2 Immune cell landscape in the Lungs of 4T1 inoculated mice 
Considering 4T1 cells can metastasize to different tissues, we chose to analyze lung samples 
for the presence of different immune cells. 
4.1.2.1 NK cells in the lungs of BALB/c mice 
NK cells are known for their capacity to eliminate metastatic cells, with a preference to single 
cell metastatic cells and cells with a more stem cell phenotype [79, 126]. Figure 15 shows the 
percentages of NK cell subsets in the lungs.  
TrIL-12 induced a more potent mobilization of NK cells to the lungs (7.25±3.16) compared to 
the HBSS treated group (3.19±0.81) (p-value=0.0166, Figure 15A). As far as the CD11b NK 
subsets are concerned, the combined treatments (Dox+TrIL-12) resulted in a higher 
percentage of CD11b- cells in the lungs (13,10±2,063) compared to chemotherapy alone 
(9.55±1.00) (p-value=0.0157, Figure 15B). Moreover, compared to HBSS (75.40±4.67) and 
doxorubicin (74.48±5.36), the TrIL-12 group demonstrated less CD11bint NK cells 
(64.76±5,39) (p-value=0.0048, Figure 15C), while as seen in Figure 15D, TrIL-12 treated 
mice displayed more CD11bhigh NK cells in the lungs (23.13±7.04) in comparison to the 
HBSS group (12.09±2.19) (p-value=0.0043).

Figure 15 Frequencies of NK cell populations in the lungs of 4T1 bearing BALB/c mice. A: percentage of 
total NK cells out of total immune cells in the lungs. B: percentage of CD11b- NK cells in the lungs. C: 
percentage of CD11bint NK cells in the lungs. D: percentage of CD11bhigh NK cells in the lungs. HBSS (n=6,

One of the tissues demonstrating TIGIT expression is the lung [34]. Thus, we also assessed 
the TIGIT expressing frequencies of NK cells. Collectively, there was an increase in TIGIT 
expressing CD11b NK cell subpopulations in response to TrIL-12. The highest increase in 
TIGIT+CD11b- NK cells was seen in the group that received doxorubicin along with TrIL-12 
(10.83±3.99) compared to the HBSS (2.82±1.37) and chemotherapy (4.34±1.87) groups (p-
value=0.0002; Figure 16A). The same increase was observed in the TIGIT+CD11bint 
population (Figure 16B). Doxorubicin+TrIL-12 treated mice displayed more TIGIT 
expressing CD11bint cells (10.30±3.64) compared to HBSS (1.38±0.31) and doxorubicin 
(1.26±0.18) (p-value=0.0001; Figure 16B). This trend was also seen in the TrIL-12 
monotherapy group (8.43±5.1) as opposed to the HBSS and doxorubicin groups (Figure 
16B). Finally, similarly to the other TIGIT expressing CD11b subsets, the TIGIT +CD11bhigh 
was more present in the lungs of TrIL-12 treated mice, albeit the lack of significant difference 
(Figure 16C).

Figure 16 Frequencies of TIGIT positive NK cell populations in the lungs of 4T1 bearing BALB/c mice. A: 
percentage of total NK cells out of total immune cells in the lungs. B: percentage of TIGIT+CD11b- NK cells

In order to investigate if the increasing frequencies of TIGIT NK cell subsets correspond to an 
upregulation of TIGIT expression in response to treatment, we calculated the MFI within the 
three distinct subpopulations. For the CD11b- subset, both TrIL-12 and the doxorubicin+TrIL-
12 groups showed a significant upregulation of TIGIT (p-value=0.0001), compared to HBSS 
and chemotherapy groups (Figure 17A). The same observation was made for the CD11bint 
subset (p-value=0.0001; Figure 17B). For the CD11bhigh cells, TrIL-12 alone seemed to 
upregulate TIGIT compared to the rest of the groups (p-value=0.0039; Figure 17C). 
Collectively, this data suggests that treatment with IL-12 induces the upregulation of TIGIT 
on NK cells.

4.1.2.2 Neutrophils in the lungs of BALB/c 4T1 mice 
As was aforementioned, Engblom et al. described the accumulation of pro-tumorigenic 
Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils in the lungs [98]. So we set out to examine if our study would have 
any effect on those cells in the lungs of 4T1 bearing mice. Figure 18 shows the effect of the 
treatments on the neutrophil populations.  
The total neutrophil frequencies decreased in response to treatment, with a stronger reduction 
observed in the TrIL-12 receiving group (22.25±11.69), followed by the combinational group 
(32.68±11.46) compared to the HBSS (58,28±8,579) and chemotherapy (39.83±11.32) groups 
(p-value=0.0007; Figure 18A). The same depleting effect was seen in the Siglec-Fhigh 
population 
(HBSS:10.44±1.2, 
Doxorubicin:9.54±1.68, 
TrIL-12:4.52±2.38 
& 
Doxorubicin+TrIL-12:4.96±1.84; p-value=0.0001, Figure 18B). Interestingly, the Siglec-F- 
population 
was 
increased 
in 
response 
to 
treatment 
(HBSS:88.58±1.45, 
Doxorubicin:89.30±1.91, 
TrIL-12: 
94.21±2.55 
& 
Doxorubicin+TrIL-12:94.15±1.9; 
p-
value=0.0002, Figure 18C).

lungs of TrIL-12 treated mice (0.36±0.15) compared to the lungs of the other groups, with the 
difference between the untreated (HBSS: 0.15±0.06) and the TrIL-12 group being significant 
(p-value=0.0044; Figure 19).

4.1.3 Treatment effect on lung metastases formation 
Since 4T1 cells are able to metastasize in secondary tissues, we performed a colonogenic 
assay to the isolated lungs from each group. With this setup we aimed to investigate whether 
the treatments had any effect on the DTCs and, thus, on the metastasis formation. As 
presented in Figure 20A, the formation of metastases was inhibited in response to treatment, 
and this inhibition was statistically significant (p-value=0.0195). Figures 20B, 20C, 20D and 
20E show the stained metastatic colonies from the lung tissue.

4.1.4 Neutrophil to NK cell Ratios in the lungs 
Among cancer patients, neutrophil to lymphocyte ratios are considered indicative for the 
patient’s outcome and response to treatment [127]. To assess if there is a connection to the 
response to metastases inhibition and the immune cell landscape of the lungs, we calculated 
the neutrophil-to-NK cell ratios.  
As seen in Figure 21A to 21D, the Neutrophil to NK cell ratios went down, indicating an 
increase in NK cell subsets in the lungs and/or a decrease in the neutrophil population. 
Compared to the untreated group, TrIL-12 alone and with doxorubicin had a significant 
decrease to the cell ratios (p-value=0.0006; Figure 21A, p-value=0.001; Figure 21B, p-
value=0.0053; Figure 21C & p-value<0.0001; Figure 21D, respectively).

Figure 22 Siglec-Fhigh-to-NK cell subsets ratios in response to treatment. A: Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils to total

lungs.

administration (p-value=0.011), compared to the group that received HBSS. This might be 
correlated with the increased frequency of the CD11bhigh NK cells in the lungs of those mice.
