4.2 
Comparative study of the effect of different IL-12 formulations 
on 4T1 bearing mice: A focus on Neutrophils 
Following the previous study, we thought of evaluating the effect of different IL-12 
formulations on the 4T1 inoculated mice. For this reason we used purified murine (native) IL-
12, MSA IL-12 and the TrIL-12 construct. In this study we focused on the neutrophils. 
Figure 24 summarizes the efficacy study, with Figure 24A giving an overview of the 
experimental setup.  
Predominantly, both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 delayed the growth of the 4T1 cells, whereas 
native IL-12 seemed to have no effect, as it seemed that the tumor growth followed a similar 
pattern to the untreated group (Figure 24B).  
No signs of toxicity in response to the treatments were observed, as there were no significant 
body weight changes to baseline (Figure 24C). Moreover, the group treated with MSA IL-12 
seemed to have the smallest tumors, weight-wise (Figure 24D).  
Figures 24E, 24F, 24G and 24H show the individual tumor growth of each mouse that 
participated in this study. The growth was continuous. However, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 
decelerated the development of the primary cancer. Contrarily, native IL-12 seemed to have 
no effect on the primary tumor.

Figure 24 Effect of different IL-12 formulations on 4T1 murine cancer model. A: schematic representation 
of the experimental setup. B: mean tumor growth curves (𝒎𝒎𝟑) of untreated (red) mice and those that 
received native IL-12 (yellow), MSA_IL-12 (light green) and TrIL-12 (blue). C: relative body weight change 
to baseline of all the four groups. D: tumor weight (mg). E: individual tumor growth curves of the untreated 
group. F: individual tumor growth curves of native IL-12 treated mice. G: individual tumor growth curves 
of the MSA_IL-12 group. H: individual tumor growth curves of the TrIL-12 treated group. The error bars 
represent mean + SEM.   
4.2.1 The neutrophil phenotypic signature in the TME in response to IL-12  
On the day of the takedown, tumors, spleens and lungs were harvested for flow cytometry 
analysis. For complete phenotyping of the immune cells refer to Supplementary Figure 3. 
From a functional aspect, we gated TAN based on Arg-1 and iNOS signal. This was in an 
attempt to get a functional imprint regarding those cells, considering that Arg-1 is associated 
with an anti-inflammatory and immunosuppressive phenotype, while iNOS is hailed as a pro-
inflammatory feature [128, 129]. 
 
 
 
Ensuingly, we evaluated the presence of the pro-tumorigenic Siglec-Fhigh tumor associated 
neutrophils (TAN). In order to get into more detail regarding the phenotype of Siglec-Fhigh 
neutrophils, we included CD54 and PD-L1 along with Arg-1 and iNOS. CD54 is a marker 
that is linked to a pro-inflammatory phenotype in neutrophils (N1) in various experimental 
models [128, 130]. PD-L1 is known to induce functional anergy and apoptosis when it links 
to the PD-1 receptor on effector cells like T cells and NK cells, and as such is used to 
determine anti-inflammatory neutrophils (N2) [129, 131]. Figure 25 demonstrates the results 
of this analysis.  
Both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 had a remissive effect on the Siglec-Fhigh cells (8.12±1.91 and 
8.9±2.27, respectively) as to the untreated (12.48±3.83) and the native IL-12 (12.41±2.46) 
receiving groups. Nonetheless, the effect of the first seemed to be stronger (p-value=0.0038; 
Figure 25A). Neither Arg-1+ nor iNOS+ Siglec-Fhigh TAN showed any significant fluctuations 
in response to the IL-12 formulations (Figure 25C and Figure 25D, respectively). However, 
there was a significant increase (p-value=0.0027) in the percentage of CD54+ Siglec-Fhigh cells 
==PAGE:40==
[35] 
 
in response to both MSA IL-12 (47.00±6.94) and TrIL-12 (47.76±7.83) related to the 
untreated group (30.96±9.84) (Figure 25B). IL-12 had also an increasing effect (37.19±6.58), 
albeit not important. A rather compelling observation came from the PD-L1+ cells (Figure 
25E). All three IL-12 formulations (Native IL-12: 49,20±5,205, MSA IL-12:85,76±2,343 and 
TrIL-12:82.60±2.23) led to an increase (p-value<0.0001) in the frequency of PD-L1 positive 
Siglec-Fhigh TAN by comparison to the untreated group (29.29±3.29).

Figure 25 Frequency of Siglec-Fhigh TAN in the primary tumor bed. A: percentage of Siglec-Fhigh TAN 
within the immune cells. B: frequency of Arg-1 expressing Siglec-FhighTAN. C: frequency of CD54 
expressing Siglec-Fhigh TAN. D: Frequency of iNOS positive Siglec-Fhigh TAN. E: frequency of PD-L1 
expressing Siglec-Fhigh TAN. UT (n=8, red), Native IL-12 (n=8, orange), MSA IL-12 (n=8, yellow) & TrIL-12 
(n=8, green). Kruskal-Wallis test and Dunn’s multiple comparison test were used for the analysis of the total 
Siglec-Fhigh and the CD54 positive TAN, while one-way ANOVA with Tukey's multiple comparisons test for 
the Arg-1, iNOS and PD-L1 Siglec-Fhigh TAN in an a=0,05. The error bars represent the standard deviation 
(±SD). 
Successively, the analysis of the Siglec-F- TAN population yielded similar results (Figure 
26). MSA IL-12 administration compelled an increase (p-value=0.0078) in those cells 
compared to Native IL-12 (91.60±1.96 vs 87.30±2.61, respectively; Figure 26A). As far as 
the Arg-1 and iNOS expressing Siglec-F- TAN are concerned, none of the treatments affected 
them significantly (Figures 26B and 26D, respectively). Contrarily, MSA IL-12 (19.60±5.15) 
and TrIL-12 (18.45±4.75) promoted and increase (p-value<0.0001) in the CD54 expressing 
cells as to native IL-12 (11.06±2.51) and the untreated (8,531±3,512) mice (Figure 26C). 
Similarly to the PD-L1 expressing Siglec-Fhigh TAN, the PD-L1+Siglec-F- TAN were 
increased (p-value<0.0001) in response to treatment with the IL-12 constructs (Native IL-12: 
==PAGE:41==
[36] 
 
34.30±3.45, MSA IL-12: 65.13±5.18 & TrIL-12: 61.84±2.03), compared to the untreated 
group (20.69±2.44) (Figure 26E).

Figure 26 Frequency of Siglec-F- TAN in the primary tumor bed. A: percentage of Siglec-F- TAN within the 
immune cells. B: frequency of Arg-1 expressing Siglec-F-TAN. C: frequency of CD54 expressing Siglec-F- 
TAN. D: Frequency of iNOS positive Siglec-F- TAN. E: frequency of PD-L1 expressing Siglec-F- TAN. UT 
(n=8, red), Native IL-12 (n=8, orange), MSA IL-12 (n=8, yellow) & TrIL-12 (n=8, green). Kruskal-Wallis 
test and Dunn’s multiple comparison test were used for the analysis of the total Siglec-F- and the Arg-1 
positive TAN, while one-way ANOVA with Tukey's multiple comparisons test for the CD54, iNOS and PD-
L1 Siglec-F- TAN in an a=0,05. The error bars represent the standard deviation (±SD). 
In consideration of the plasticity that characterizes neutrophils and also the discovery of some 
neutrophils with antigen-presenting properties in lung cancer patients [132-134], we identified 
a neutrophil subset that expressed MHC-II in our 4T1 model. As seen in Figure 27A, there 
was a slight increase in the percentage of APC-like TAN in response to TrIL-12, even though 
it was not of significant importance. Strikingly, the IL-12 regimens induced an escalation (p-
value<0.0001) in the CD54+ APC-like TAN (MSA IL-12: 32,29±6,776 & TrIL-12: 
28,68±4,690) as to the untreated (15.93±4.94) and the native IL-12 treated (20.64±3.91) 
groups (Figure 27B). There was a fluctuation in the Arg-1 and iNOS expressing cells, but no 
significant difference was noticed (Figures 27C and 27E). Lastly, there was a clear increase 
(p-value<0.0001) in the CD54+ APC-like TAN that expressed PD-L1 in the MSA IL-12 
(85.75±0.99) and TrIL-12 (82.89±3.59) treated groups, compared to the untreated 
(45.28±6.99) and the native IL-12 (63.73±6.64) groups (Figure 27D).

Figure 27 Frequency of APC-like TAN in the primary tumor bed. A: percentage of APC-like TAN within 
the immune cells. B: frequency of CD54 expressing APC-like TAN. C: frequency of Arg-1 expressing CD54+ 
APC-like TAN. D: Frequency of PD-L1 positive CD54+ APC-like TAN. E: frequency of iNOS expressing

Our next step was to evaluate the expression of CD54, iNOS, Arg-1 and PD-L1 to the pro-
tumorigenic Siglec-Fhigh TAN population. CD54 expression was upregulated in response to 
MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 compared to the control (untreated) group (p-value=0.0092; Figure 
28A). MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 appeared to mediate iNOS upregulation too compared to the 
other two groups (p-value=0.0007; Figure 28B). The same effect was observed in relation to 
Arg-1 expression (p-value=0.0018; Figure 28C). Last but not least, PD-L1 upregulation was 
seen in response to TrIL-12, but a higher expression was seen in response to MSA IL-12 (p-
value<0.0001; Figure 28D).

Figure 28 Expression profiling of the Siglec-Fhigh TAN in response to treatment. A: CD54 expression. B: 
iNOS expression. C: Arg-1 expression. D: PD-L1 expression. UT (n=8, red), Native IL-12 (n=8, orange), 
MSA IL-12 (n=8, yellow), TrIL-12 (n=8, green). The expression is calculated as the mean fluorescence

comparison tests, while iNOS data were analyzed with one-way ANOVA and Tuckey’s multiple comparison 
tests in an a=0,05. 
The same MFI based expression analysis was applied for the Siglec-F- TAN. Figure 29A 
shows an increasing trend in the CD54 expression by the Siglec-F- TAN in response to IL-12, 
with TrIL-12 having a significant upregulating effect  (p-value=0.0042) compared to the 
control mice. Upregulation of iNOS was also seen in response to MSA IL-12 (p-
value=0.0174) compared to native IL-12 (Figure 29B). There was an upregulating trend to 
Arg-1 in response to treatment (p-value=0.0056; Figure 29C), though multiple comparisons 
did not reveal any differences between the groups. PD-L1 expression, however, was 
significantly increased as a response to MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 (p-value<0.0001), by 
comparison to the control and native IL-12 groups (Figure 29D).

Figure 29 Expression profiling of Siglec-F- TAN. A: CD54 expression. B: iNOS expression. C: Arg-1

Lastly, we examined the expression of Arg-1, iNOS and PD-L1 on the CD54+ APC-like 
TAN. Treatment with the IL-12 constructs seemed to induced an upregulating trend to iNOS 
expressed by the CD54+ APC-like TAN (p-value=0.0238; Figure 30A). Regarding Arg-1, 
MSA IL-12 induced a higher expression in those cells (p-value=0.0255; Figure 30B). Finally, 
all three IL-12 treatments upregulated PD-L1 expression by the APC-like TAN (p-
value<0.0001) compared to the control group, with MSA IL-12 performing a little better 
(Figure 30C).

Figure 30 Expression profiling of CD54+ APC-like TAN. A: iNOS expression. B:Arg-1 expression. C: PD-L1 
expression. UT (n=8, red), Native IL-12 (n=8, orange), MSA IL-12 (n=8, yellow), TrIL-12 (n=8, green). The

Knowing that frequencies might not always be representative of the infiltration of the immune 
cells to the tumor, we determined the number of TAN populations per gram of tumor. By 
doing so we had a clearer estimation of the tumor infiltrating neutrophils. As it is summarized 
in Figure 31, MSA IL-12 led to the lowest TAN infiltration across all of the populations 
(Figures 31A to 31D). The count f Siglec-Fhigh TAN was significantly lower (p-
value=0.0331) in this group, suggesting a possible exclusion of those cells from the primary 
tumor in response to treatment. Even though it was not significant, TrIL-12 seemed to induce 
the infiltration of APC-like neutrophils in the TME (Figure 31D).

Figure 31 TAN population infiltration in the TME. A: Total TAN count in the TME. B: Count of Siglec-

4.2.2 The neutrophil phenotypic signature in the lungs in response to IL-12 
Apart from the effect of the treatments on the TME, we also evaluated their effect on the lung 
neutrophils. Treatment with MSA IL-12 (55.18±9.82) and especially TrIL-12 (47.35±13.70) 
induced a decrease in the frequencies of neutrophils in the lungs compared to mice that were 
not treated (68.96±13.33) or the ones received native IL-12 (77.03±5.92) (p-value=0.0013; 
Figure 32A). No significant difference was noticeable among the groups regarding the 
percentage of Arg-1+ neutrophils (Figure 32B). Interesting enough was the notable increase 
of iNOS+ neutrophils (p-value=0.0465) in the lungs of TrIL-12 (46.96±24.67) injected mice 
as opposed to the ones that were injected with MSA IL-12 (12.70±13.37) (Figure 32C).

Figure 32 Frequency of lung neutrophils in response to treatment in 4T1 bearing mice. A: frequency of total 
neutrophils. B: percentage of Arg-1+ neutrophils. C: percentage of iNOS+ neutrophils. UT (n=8, red), Native

multiple comparison test were used for the analysis of this dataset, in an a=0,05. The error bars represent

In regard to Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils in the lungs, treatment with TrIL-12 (6.48±1.90) and 
MSA IL-12 (5.65±2.43) reduced their frequency (p-value=0.0023) compared to the untreated 
mice (12.86±3.50) (Figure 33A). Figures 33B, 33C and 33D representing Arg-1, CD54 and 
iNOS expressing Siglec-Fhigh lung neutrophils, respectively, show that there was a fluctuation 
in the frequency of those cells in the different groups. However, there was no noticeable 
significant difference. The PD-L1+ cells, nonetheless, were significantly increased (p-
value<0.0001) in response to MSA IL-12 (83.57±7.66) and TrIL-12 (87.53±9.87), as to the 
untreated (3.86±1,86) and the group that received native IL-12 (10.05±6.15) (Figure 33E).

As for the Siglec-F- lung neutrophils, MSA IL-12 (94.25±2.38) and TrIL-12 (93.27±2.06)

comparison to the untreated (3.17±1.75) and the native IL-12 groups (7.39±4.50) (Figure 
34E).

4.2.3 Metastatic lesions in the lungs in response to IL-12 
Ultimately, we performed a colony formation assay to check for metastatic lesions in the 
lungs of the mice. Figure 38A shows that all the IL-12 formulations had an anti-metastatic 
effect, with the MSA IL-12 and the TrIL-12 having a stronger result (p-value=0.0012). 
Figures 38B, 38C, 38D and 38E present the stained colonies from the lungs in the HBSS 
(untreated), Native IL-12, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 groups, respectively.

Figure 38 Effect of different IL-12 regimens on the metastatic lesion formation in the lungs of 4T1 bearing 
mice. A: statistical analysis of metastasis formation in response to treatment. B: stained colonies in the lungs 
of untreated mice. C: stained colonies in the lungs of native IL-12 treated mice. D: stained colonies in the

red), Native IL-12 (n=8, orange), MSA IL-12 (n=8, yellow), TrIL-12 (n=8, green). The colonogenic assay 
analysis was done with Kruskal-Wallis test with Dunn’s multiple comparisons tests in an a=0,05. The error 
bars represent the standard deviation (±SD).  
4.3 Comparative study of the effect of different IL-12 formulations on 
4T1 bearing mice: A NK cell perspective  
Next, we proceeded with evaluating the effect of different IL-12 formulations on the 4T1 
inoculated mice. For this reason we used purified murine (native) IL-12, MSA IL-12 and the 
TrIL-12 construct, but with a focus on NK cells. Figure 39 summarizes the efficacy study, 
with Figure 39A giving an overview of the experimental setup.  
Predominantly, both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 delayed the growth of the 4T1 cells, whereas 
native IL-12 seemed to have a lesser effect, as it seemed that the tumor growth followed a 
similar pattern to the untreated group (Figure 39B).  
No signs of toxicity in response to the treatments were observed, as there were no significant 
changes in the body weight (Figure 39C). Moreover, the group treated with MSA IL-12 
seemed to have the smallest tumors, weight-wise (Figure 39D).  
Figures 39E, 39F, 39G and 39H show the individual tumor growth of each mouse that 
participated in this study. The growth was continuous. However, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 
decelerated the development of the primary cancer. Contrarily, native IL-12 seemed to have 
little effect on the primary tumor.

Figure 39 Effect of different IL-12 formulations on 4T1 murine cancer model. A: schematic representation 
of the experimental setup. B: mean tumor growth curves (𝒎𝒎𝟑) of untreated (red) mice and those that

4.3.1 NK cells & subtypes in the primary tumor 
The focus of this study was to identify NK cell subsets in the TME in response to different 
IL-12 regiments. Within the major NK cell population we identified three subpopulations 
based on the CD11b and CD27 expression. These subsets were the CD11b-CD27-, the 
CD11bintCD27- and the CD11bhighCD27-. Unusually, we did not identify any CD27+ NK cells 
in the TME (for complete immune phenotyping see Supplementary Figures 4, 5, 6 & 7). 
No significant difference was observed in the percentage of total NK cells among the 
treatment groups as it is apparent in Figure 40A (UT:13.66±4.67, Native IL-12:16,98±9,446, 
MSA IL-12:13.07±3.66 & TrIL-12:16.05±4.55). A decreasing trend was observed in the 
frequency of CD11b-CD27- NK cells in response to treatment (UT:4.65±2.36, Native IL-
12:3.68±1.27, MSA IL-12:3.82±1.57 & TrIL-12:3.78±1.51; Figure 40B). Similarly, 
CD11bintCD27- frequencies were decreased, albeit not significantly (UT:31,79±3,627, Native 
IL-12:26.99±6,31, MSA IL-12:25.99±4.52 & TrIL-12:25.45±3.67; Figure 40C). The 
percentages of the CD11bhighCD27- subset were significantly higher (p-value=0.0034) in 
Native IL-12 (67.64±5.80), MSA IL-12 (68.81±3.85) and TrIL-12 (69.30±3.80) compared to 
the untreated group (61.51±3.03) (Figure 40D).

Figure 40 Main NK cell subsets in the TME in response to IL-12 based treatments. A: Frequency of total 
NK cells in the primary tumor. B: frequency of CD11b-CD27- NK cells in the TME. C: frequency of 
CD11bintCD27- NK cells in the TME. D: frequency of CD11bhighCD27- NK cells in the TME. UT (n=8,

To explore the functionality and activation of the NK cells in the primary tumor bed, we gated 
them based on IFN-γ, GzmB, CD25 and TIM-3 [136, 137].  
The expression levels of IFN-g within the total NK cell population was rather uniform among 
the groups as depicted from the MFI analysis (Figure 41A). Regarding the GzmB expression, 
there was a significant change among the groups (p-value=0.0235). However the multiple 
comparisons failed to unveil the differences (Figure 41B). Finally, the CD25 expression was 
significantly upregulated in response to all three IL-12 formulations compared to the 
untreated group (p-value=0.0052; Figure 41C). TIM-3 expression was upregulated in the 
MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 groups in comparison to the HBSS (UT) group (p-value=0.0137, 
Figure 41D).

addition of the CXCR3 receptor to assess the potentially migrating NK cell subsets, as 
presented in the following chapters.  
4.3.1.1 The CD11b-CD27- NK cell subset 
The frequency of CXCR3+CD11b-CD27- NK cells was low, and showed a fluctuation among 
the groups (UT:0.97±0.87, Native IL-12:1.37±1.32, MSA IL-12:0.92±0.53 & TrIL-
12:2.06±1.87), even if not significant (Figure 42A). However, the frequency of GzmB+ cells 
was significantly lower (p-value=0.0252) in the TrIL-12 group (16.26±14.39) compared to 
the Native IL-12 group (38.40±18.11), whereas no significant differences were observed as 
far as the other groups are concerned (UT:28.92±21.09 & MSA IL-12:16.72±11.78; Figure 
42B). Regarding the percentages of IFN-g+, TIM-3+ and CD25+ CD11b-CD27- cells, no 
significant changes were noticeable, though there was a decreasing motif in the IFN-g 
subtype (Figure 42C), and an increasing one in the TIM-3 and CD25 expressing cells 
(Figures 42D & 42E, respectively).

Kruskal-Wallis test and Dunn’s multiple comparison test were used for the analysis of this dataset in an 
a=0,05.  
4.3.1.2 The CD11bintCD27- NK cell subset 
The CXCR3+CD11bintCD27- populations were akin among the groups (Figure 44A). The 
GzmB+CD11bintCD27- cells were diminished in the MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 groups as 
opposed to UT and Native IL-12 groups (Figure 44B), although not significantly. IFN-g+ 
cells were progressively lower in response to treatments, albeit not significantly (Figure 
44C). The proportion of CD25 expressing cells did not display any outstanding changes 
(Figure 44D), while TIM-3 expressing cells were slightly more frequent in the MSA IL-12 
group, though not significantly higher (Figure 44E).

was lower in the MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 groups compared to the Native IL-12 group (p-
value=0.0059; Figure 45B). Finally, CD25 expression was rather identical among the groups 
(Figure 45C).

4.3.1.3 Split-anergy in NK cells in the TME 
Split-anergy has been identified as a state in which NK cells downregulate or lose their 
cytotoxic activity and simultaneously increase the cytokine production like IFN-g [138, 139]. 
TIM-3 has been associated with the induction of split-anergy, and in particular with enhanced 
IFN-g production [140, 141].  
IFN-g expression was higher in the TIM-3 expressing NK cells in comparison with the TIM-3 
negative cells, and it was observed in all of the four groups (p-value=0.0148, Figure 46A; p-
value=0.0023, Figure 46B; p-value=0.0007, Figure 46C & p-value=0.003, Figure 46D). 
GzmB expression was also higher in the TIM-3+ NK cells as opposed to the TIM-3- cells (p-
value=0.0499, Figure 46A; p-value=0.007, Figure 46B; p-value=0.0003, Figure 46C & p-
value=0.0006, Figure 46D).

4.3.1.4 Tumor-Infiltrating NK cells (TINK) 
Admittedly, there was an increase in the infiltration of NK cells in the primary tumor stroma 
in response to IL-12 formulations (Figure 47). Although the statistical analysis did not 
uncover any significant difference among the groups, there was a noticeable increase in the 
TINK present in the TME responding to TrIL-12.

4.3.2 NK cells & subtypes in the lungs 
Secondly, we assessed the frequencies of NK cells in the lungs. 
Total NK cells were more frequent in the lungs of the mice that received MSA IL-12 
(11.11±2.56) and TrIL-12 (11.80±2.25) compared to the untreated (6.54±1.51) and the group 
receiving native IL-12 (5.49±1.21) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 48A). Figure 48B demonstrates 
that the CD11bintCD27- cells were significantly lesser in all of the IL-12 treated mice (Native 
IL-12: 5.75±1.38, MSA IL-12:6.18±2.47 & TrIL-12:6.40±1.13) as to the untreated group 
(10.20±4.37) (p-value=0.009). Similarly, the CD11bintCD27- cells were lower in the MSA IL-
12 and TrIL-12 (52.39±3.45 and 56.61±3.93, respectively) in comparison to the untreated and 
native IL-12 groups (71.13±6.28 and 74.29±4.44, respectively) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 
48C). Finally, there was an increase in the percentage of CD11bhighCD27- NK subset in 
response to MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 (38.47±3.20 and 34.50±4.34, respectively), whereas 
these cells were less frequent in the untreated and the native IL-12 groups (17.00±2.08 & 
18.28±3.79, respectively) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 48D).

the lungs of the 4T1 bearing mice in the different groups (Figures 49C & 49D, respectively).

On the functional status of the NK cells, MSA IL-12 induced the higher effector functions as

(Figure 50A & 50B, respectively). The activation of the NK cells was reflected on the 
expression of CD25. As it is presented in Figure 50C, MSA IL-12 induced the greatest 
activation compared to the murine IL-12 and the TrIL-12 (p-value<0.0001).

4.3.2.1 The CD11b-CD27- NK cell subset 
TrIL-12 (20.66±14.29) and MSA IL-12 (16.59±8.78) increased the frequency of the CD25+ 
cells within the CD11b-CD27- subset (p-value=0.0029; Figure 51A) by comparison to the 
untreated group (5.86±3.65). A similar increase was seen in the CXCR3+ cluster 
(UT:6.04±3.88, MSA IL-12:19.02±10.19 & TrIL-12:23.28±14.29) (p-value=0.0012; Figure 
51B). The GzmB expressing cells were also increased in response to MSA IL-12 
(30.01±8.86) and TrIL-12 (23.58±14.70) compared to the untreated (6.23±4.14) and the 
murine IL-12 (9.32±5.33) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 51C). In response to the MSA IL-12, we 
noticed the higher increase in the frequency of the IFN-g+ cells (5.00±2.06) by contrast to the 
untreated group that had the lowest frequency (1.46±0.67) (p-value=0.0192; Figure 51D). 
Finally, the highest percentage of TIM-3+CD11b-CD27- NK cells were seen in response to 
TrIL-12 (10.49±6.96 vs UT: 2.46±1.71) (p-value=0.0035; Figure 51E).

4.3.2.2 The CD11bintCD27- NK cell subset 
As seen in Figure 53A, no significant changes were noticeable among the groups in the 
CD25+CD11bintCD27- lung NK cells. The CXCR3+CD11bintCD27- cells were increased in the 
MSA IL-12 (19.61±3.97) comparison to the untreated mice (11.42±2.63) (p-value=0.0131; 
Figure 53B). The GzmB+ cells were more frequent in the MSA IL-12 mice (49.10±18.72) by 
contrast to the untreated (12.89±2.95) and the TrIL-12 (18.93±11.40) (p-value=0.0006; 
Figure 62C). Surprisingly, the most significant difference in the IFN-g+ cells was noticed in 
the MSA IL-12 (7.75±4.43) and the TrIL-12 (2.36±2.38) (p-value=0.0255; Figure 53D). No 
significant differences were observed in regards to the TIM-3+ population among the groups 
(Figure 53E).

4.3.2.3 The CD11bhighCD27- NK cell subset 
The frequency of the activated CD11bhighCD27- cells (CD25+) was higher in the untreated and 
the Native IL-12 groups (3.66±0.92 & 3.58±0.82, respectively) compared to the MSA IL-12 
and the TrIL-12 (1.22±0.29 & 1.21±0.49, respectively) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 55A). There 
was a decline in the CXCR3+ subset, but of no significance (Figure 55B). The 
CD11bhighCD27- cell with a cytotoxic potential (GzmB+) were more frequent in the MSA IL-
12 (53.77±21.01) in proportion to the other groups (UT:17.54±4.427, Native IL-
12:23.49±6.58 & TrIL-12:15.25±11.43, correspondingly) (p-value<0.0001; Figure 55C). 
IFN-g+CD11bhighCD27- cells were more abundant in the untreated mice (20.95±8.00), and 
displayed a progressive decline (p-value=0.0005) in response to the different IL-12 
formulations (Native IL-12:15.56±7.32, MSA IL-12:10.52±4.19 & TrIL-12:5.95±5.05, 
correspondingly) (Figure 55D). A similar observation was made in the TIM-3+ cells 
(UT:4.25±1.05, Native IL-12:3.71±1.16, MSA IL-12:3.03±0.85 & TrIL-12:2.12±0.69, 
respectively) (p-value=0.001, Figure 55E).

4.3.3 Metastatic lesions in the lungs in response to IL-12 
Eventually, we performed a colony formation assay to check for metastatic lesions in the 
lungs of the mice. Figure 57A shows that all the IL-12 formulations had an anti-metastatic 
effect, with the MSA IL-12 and the TrIL-12 having a stronger result (p-value=0.0002). 
Figures 57B, 57C, 57D and 57E present the stained colonies from the lungs in the HBSS 
(untreated), Native IL-12, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 groups, respectively.

4.4 Neutrophil depletion & TrIL-12 treatment 
 4T1 cells and neutrophils have a rather interactive relationship. G-CSF secreted by the tumor 
cells facilitates the recruitment of neutrophils in the TME and in the pre-metastatic niche 
[142, 143]. In turn, the latter promote their growth and metastasis [87]. In respect to this, we 
decided to deplete the neutrophils by means of an anti-Ly6G antibody, while comparing the 
effect of the TrIL-12 in both depleted and non-depleted mice.  
Figure 58A is a graphical abstract of the experimental setup. Figure 58B shows the tumor 
growth in the different groups. As it seemed, the HBSS treated mice had a continuous tumor 
growth, while the HBSS mice that also received the aLy6G had a slower tumor growth. TrIL-
12 i.v. treatment delayed tumor growth, but the s.c. administration had a stronger effect. 
Strikingly, TrIL-12 with simultaneous aLy6G treatment had the strongest effect on the 4T1 
tumor growth, as they demonstrated a profound adjournment in the tumor progression. 
Additionally, no signs of toxicity were detected by monitoring the weight of each individual 
mouse (Figure 58C). Figures 58D, 58E, 58F, 58G and 58H illustrate the individual tumor 
growths of each mouse.

4.4.1Immune cell composition in the lungs of 4T1 implanted mice 
On the day of the takedown, we assessed the immune cell populations in the lungs (for 
immunophenotyping gating see Supplementary Figures 8, 9 & 10). For technical issues, we 
only analyzed the HBSS, TrIL-12 i.v. group and the TrIL-12 s.c. group.  
4.4.1.1 Neutrophils  
Similar to our previous observations, a decrease in the neutrophil population was observed in 
the lungs of the TrIL-12 mice that received it i.v., compared to the untreated. S.c. injection 
seemed to have a lesser effect on neutrophils compared to the i.v. administration 
(HBSS:69.80±5.42, TrIL-12i.v.:57.23±27.89 & TrIL-12s.c.:67.60±9.33; Figure 59A). 
Accordingly, there was a reduction in the frequency of Sglec-Fhigh neutrophils in the lungs of 
the mice that received the TrIL-12 i.v. and to a less extent s.c. as to the untreated mice 
(HBSS:15.70±4.65, TrIL-12i.v.:11.46±4.08 & TrIL-12s.c.:12.76±2.18; Figure 59B). as slight 
increase in the Siglec-F- neutrophils in response to TrIL-12i.v. & s.c. (HBSS:80.07±6.28, 
TrIL-12i.v.:84.40±3.78 & TrIL-12s.c.: 84.04±3.97; Figure 59C). Finally, there was an 
increase in the APC-like neutrophils in response to TrIL-12 by comparison to HBSS 
(HBSS:21.83±8.87, TrIL-12i.v.:26.53±1.98 & TrIL-12s.c.:26.17±2.50; Figure 59D), albeit of 
no significant difference.

Figure 59 Neutrophil subsets in the lungs of 4T1 inoculated mice. A: frequency of neutrophils within the 
lung immune cells. B: frequency of Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils. C: frequency of Siglec-F- neutrophils. D: 
frequency of the APC-like neutrophils. HBSS (n=6, light blue), TrIL-12i.v. (n=7, violet) & TrIL-12s.c. (n=7, 
pink). Kruskal-Wallis test and Dunn’s multiple comparison test were used for the analysis of this dataset in 
an a=0,05. The error bars represent the standard deviation (±SD). 
As a functional imprint, we estimated the frequency of the IFN-g+ neutrophils in the lungs, in 
an attempt to see if there were any pro-inflammatory neutrophils in mice, as an IFN-g 
producing neutrophil population was described in humans, and the production was after IL-12 
stimulation [91].  
The frequency of IFN-g+ neutrophils was lower in the lungs of treated mice, compared to the 
lungs of the untreated ones (Figure 59A). In accordance, the presence of INF-g producing 
APC-neutrophil hybrids and Siglec-F- neutrophils was reduced in both TrIL-12 treated groups 
compared to the untreated mice (Figure 59B & Figure 59C). Nonetheless, these observations 
were not significantly different.

4.4.1.2 NK cells  
Figure 62 summarizes the investigated data regarding NK cells in the metastatic lungs. 
Though not significant, the frequency of total NK cells was slightly higher in the i.v. TrIL-12 
==PAGE:64==
[59] 
 
treated mice (Figure 62A). The CD11b- NK cell subset was reduced in the s.c. TrIL-12 
treated mice (Figure 62B), albeit not significantly. Similarly, the CD11bint NK cells were 
reduced in the i.v. administered TrIL-12 group (Figure 62C). On the other hand, the 
frequency of CD11bhigh NK cells was increased in response to TrIL-12, with the i.v. injection 
yielding slightly higher percentages (Figure 62D). Interestingly, the frequency of IFN-g+ total 
NK cells was higher in the untreated group (Figure 62E). The presence of NKp46+ NK cells 
was strongly detected in the lungs of the i.v. TrIL-12 treated mice (Figure 62F). Significant 
was the reduction in the IFN-g+ NKp46+ NK cells in the lungs of mice treated with TrIL-12 
i.v. (14.61±2.40) compared to the HBSS treated group (25.98±9.41) (p-value=0.0236; Figure 
62G).  Finally, relatively similar where the percentages of the NKp46- NK cells in the lungs 
of all the mice in the different groups (Figure 62H), while a reduction in the IFN-g+ NKp46- 
NK cells was seen in response to TrIL-12 (Figure 62I), though not significant.

Contrarily to the CD11b- subset, the CD11bintIFN-g+ NK cells were decreased in the lungs of 
the TrIL-12 administered mice (Figure 65A). The NKp46+CD11bint cluster was increased in 
following TrIL-12 i.v. administration, albeit not significantly (Figure 65B). surprisingly, the 
frequencies of the IFN-g+NKp46+CD11bint NK cells were significantly lower in the TrIL-12 
treated lungs, as opposed to the untreated mice (HBSS:54.08±12.29, TrIL-12 i.v.:34.91±5.29 
& TrIL-12 s.c.:36.87±5.93; p-value=0.0038) (Figure 65C).

4.4.2 Metastatic colonies in the lungs of untreated & TrIL-12 treated mice 
Finally, to determine if the way of administration affects the anti-metastatic effect of TrIL-12, 
we performed a metastasis formation assay from the isolated lungs of the HBSS, TrIL-12 i.v. 
and the TrIL-12 s.c. treated groups. Figure 69A shoes that the TrIL-12 administered both i.v. 
and s.c. induce metastasis clearance compared to the untreated mice (p-value=0,0002), with 
the i.v. injection performing slightly better.  Figures 69B, 69C and 69D display the stained 
colonies that were formed in the lungs of the 4T1 inoculated mice.

4.4.3 Neutrophil-to-NK cell ratios in the lungs 
Contemplating on the interplay between neutrophils and NK cells during the events of 
metastases, we aimed to investigate the ratios of different neutrophil and NK cell population 
in the lungs. 
4.4.3.1 Neutrophil-to-NK cell Ratios 
Figure 70 outlines the ratios of different neutrophil subtypes to the total NK cells. The total 
Nφ/ΝΚ cell ratio was not significantly different among the three groups (Figure 70A). 
Similarly, no important changes were observed in the Siglec-Fhigh and Siglec-F- to total NK 
cells ratios (Figure 70B & 70C). Still, an increase to the APC-like neutrophils to NK cell 
ratio was seen between the HBSS and the TrIL-12 s.c. groups (p-value=0.0383; Figure 70D).

4.4.3.2 Neutrophil to NKp46+ NK cell Ratios

73B & 73C, respectively). No changes in the total Nφ and APC-like Nφ to the 
aforementioned NK cell subset was observed (Figures 73A & 73D, respectively).

4.5 Effect of different treatments on the overall survival of 4T1 bearing 
mice 
As a final chapter for this research, we decided to perform an efficacy study, with a focus on 
the overall survival of the mice. For this reason we inoculated mice with 4T1 cells and we 
assessed the effect of doxorubicin, native IL-12, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 on the tumor 
growth and the survival of the subjects included in this experiment.  
4.5.1 Efficacy 
Figure 74 summarizes the efficacy study, with Figure 74A encapsulating the experimental 
setup.  
Both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 delayed the growth of the 4T1 cells, whereas doxorubicin and 
native IL-12 seemed to have a weaker effect, as it seemed that the tumor growth followed a 
similar pattern to the untreated (HBSS) group (Figure 74B).  
No signs of toxicity in response to the treatments were observed, as there were no significant 
changes in the body weight (Figure 74C).  
Figures 74D, 74E, 74F, 74G and 74H show the individual tumor growth of each mouse. The 
growth was continuous. However, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 decelerated the development of 
the primary cancer. Contrarily, native IL-12 seemed to have little effect on the primary tumor.

4.5.2 Overall survival (OS) of 4T1 inoculated mice in response to treatments 
To assess the effect of the treatments on the survival of the tumor bearing mice, we generated 
a Kapplan-Meier graph, as presented in Figure 75. As the primary time point (day 0), we 
selected the day of the 4T1 inoculation. Despite a slight survival advantage of the TrIL-12 
treated mice over the other groups, the statistical analysis failed to detect a significant change 
in the median survival.

5. Discussion 
NK cells have been deployed in the fight against cancer due to their unique character. They 
comprise the lymphoid cells if the innate immune system that can eradicate tumor cells via a 
simple ligand-activating receptor interaction, bypassing the need for antigen recognition 
[126]. This effect is seen especially in tumor lacking MHC-I or tumor that are MHC-Ilow, 
which are malignancies known to escape from the T cell control, but succumb to NK cells 
[146, 147]. However, despite their advantage over T cell that need prior sensitization, there 
are some tumors like invasive breast cancer that are associated with a low count of NK cell 
infiltration and poor prognosis [136]. This NK cell exclusion from the tumor bed is partially 
due to the differential expression of inhibitory receptors, which upon interaction with their 
respective ligands lead the NK cells to anergy or apoptosis [118, 136]. Another reason is the 
phenotypic alterations occurring on the NK cells as a result of the immunosuppressive TME 
[148]. Thus, there is a need for a strategy development to overcome this obstacle in cancer 
treatment. The majority of these strategies include modulation of the NK cell activity by 
activating cytokines like IL-12, IL-15 and IL-18, as well as the use of immune checkpoint 
inhibitors [148, 149]. This project set to explore the effect of different IL-12 based regimens 
on the NK cells, and their ability to modulate 4T1 tumor growth and clearance. In addition, 
we aimed to explore the relationship of the aforementioned cells with another population of 
the innate immunity, the neutrophils, especially in the context of metastasis.  
==PAGE:72==
[67] 
 
5.1 Effect of Chemotherapy and TrIL-12: towards a synergistic effect? 
Our first trial was based on the administration of doxorubicin and TrIL-12 as monotherapies 
and as a combination. Doxorubicin was chosen since it is considered a standard of care for 
breast cancer (fitting for the 4T1 tumor model) [119]. Moreover, we aimed to see if the co-
administration of TrIL-12 with the chemotherapy would induce a stronger anti-tumor and 
anti-metastatic effect. It has been shown that combination of chemotherapy with IL-12 has a 
synergistic effect in treating HER2+ metastatic cancers [150]. As seen in Figure 8B, we did 
not detect a strong synergistic effect of doxorubicin with the TrIL-12, compared to the TrIL-
12 as monotherapy, but it seemed that the TrIL-12 performed better than the chemotherapy 
alone. This was noted by the delay in the primary tumor growth. To address this effect, we 
analyzed the frequency of the NK cells in the tumor, and as seen in Figure 9, in response to 
TrIL-12 we had less CD11b- immature NK cells, and more CD11bint and CD11bhigh mature 
NK cells. Knowing how IL-12 activation may lead to an eventual exhaustion, we looked for 
TIGIT expressing NK cells in the TME [34-36, 118, 141]. We did not detect any CD11b-
TIGIT+ NK cells in the TME, and the frequency of the TIGIT+CD11bhigh NK cells was lower 
in response to TrIL-12 and doxorubicin, compared to the untreated group (Figure 9), whereas 
from a functional endpoint, IL-12 did not seem to induce upregulation of TIGIT (Figure 10). 
Surprisingly, a greater total NK cell infiltration was noted in the doxorubicin treated tumors 
(Figure 11).  
Next, we assessed the effect of the treatments on neutrophils, considering 4T1 tumors are 
characterized by neutrophilia. Specifically, we aimed to see the effect of the treatment on a 
pro-tumorigenic neutrophil subset hailed by the expression of Siglec-F (Siglec-Fhigh 
neutrophils) [96, 151]. Although there was a decreasing trend in the frequency of those cells, 
a stronger difference was seen in the number of the infiltrating Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils in 
response to TrIL-12 as a monotherapy (Figure 13), suggesting that TrIL-12 may have a 
modulating effect on pro-tumorigenic neutrophils. An interesting finding was the discovery of 
a neutrophil cluster that recognized the AH-1 specific Dextramer (Figure 14, Supplementary 
Figure 17). As it was previously mentioned, dextramers constitute polymers that carry MHC-
I molecules conjugated with fluorophores and they are primary used to identify CD8 specific 
T cells [123]. This means that the polymer will react with a T cell via the TCR-antigen 
recognition. According to the literature, there is a neutrophil subset (in both humans and 
mice) that is able to express a variable TCRαβ immunoreceptor [124, 125]. We hypothesize 
that this TCR+ neutrophil population is also present in our model, and that in fact it is an anti-
tumorigenic or pro-inflammatory population. A possible mechanism may lie in the 
endogenous capacity of neutrophils to take up debris or other cells. Neutrophils are 
professional phagocytes [94, 152], and perhaps in the TME they eat up tumor cells at an early 
stage during the tumor progression, then process the material they have phagocytosed and 
present it or in the case of the TCR expressing cluster, load it onto their immunoreceptor. 
Nonetheless, this needs further investigation.  
Acknowledging the important role of NK cells in controlling metastasis, we investigated the 
effect of our treatments in lung metastasis. Indeed, we observed more NK cells and CD11bhigh 
mature NK cells in the lungs of the mice treated with TrIL-12 (Figure 15). In this case we 
also noticed more TIGIT+ NK cell subsets (Figure 16) and higher TIGIT expression in 
response to TrIL-12 (Figure 17), consistent with the literature, suggesting activation and 
subsequent exhaustion mediated by IL-12 [112, 118, 153]. Moreover, we detected a decrease 
in the Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils (Figure 18) and an increase in the AH-1 specific neutrophil 
subset (Figure 19), supporting our hypothesis of the TrIL-12 in modulating neutrophils.  
To further assess the anti-metastatic effect of the TrIL-12, we did a metastasis formation 
assay. TrIL-12 administration helped in clearing the metastases in the lungs of the mice 
(Figure 20).  This effect seemed to be mediated by the increased presence of NK cells in the 
lungs and the decreased frequency of the neutrophils, as we detected decreased neutrophil to 
NK cell ratios in the lungs of the TrIL-12 treated mice (Figures 21 & 22). These results are 
==PAGE:73==
[68] 
 
consistent with the current reports stating the clinical importance of neutrophil to lymphocyte 
ratios and their relevance to clinical outcome [4, 127, 154]. 
 
5.2 IL-12 based regimens and neutrophils: unraveling the paradox 
Neutrophils constitute a population of myeloid cells with multiple roles in health and disease. 
There is not a clear consensus on whether neutrophils can be actually distinguished in two 
populations termed N1 or pro-inflammatory and N2 or anti-inflammatory/ pro-tumorigenic, 
since those states are influenced by the surrounding milieu. Moreover, functionally 
neutrophils resemble the PMN-MDSCs and vice versa, meaning that their exact 
characterization is quite a hustle [84, 155, 156]. Furthermore, certain reports stated the effect 
of IL-12 in modulating MDSCs and skewing their phenotype towards a more mature state, 
resulting in a favorable outcome in terms of tumor control [157, 158]. Consequently, we 
proceeded with investigating the effect of different IL-12 formulations of the phenotype of 
neutrophils.   
We tested three different forms of IL-12: murine (native) IL-12, MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12. We 
demonstrated that both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 performed better than the native IL-12 in 
delaying the tumor growth compared to the untreated group, with no signs of toxicity (Figure 
24). Afterwards we continued with a TME investigation with a focus on neutrophils. CD54 
has been hailed as a marker that identifies N1 neutrophils [128, 130, 159], so we used it in our 
4T1 model. For N2 we used PD-L1 [85, 87, 88]. Moreover we used iNOS and Arg-1, since 
iNOS mediated NO production is linked to a pro-inflammatory state [155, 160], while Arg-1 
is linked to an immunosuppressive phenotype [131]. In our model we failed to see a clear 
CD54+ population, whereas it was expressed on both Siglec-Fhigh (pro-tumorigenic) and the 
Siglec-F- neutrophils (Supplementary Figure 3). Consistent with our previous results, 
Siglec-Fhigh TANs were decreased in response to IL-12, but the CD54 and PD-L1 expressing 
clusters were increased (Figure 25). Contrarily, the Siglec-F- TANs increased with the CD54 
and PD-L1 clusters within them following the same trend (Figure 26). From a functional 
point of view, TANs in the MSA IL-12 treated mice seemed to have higher iNOS expression 
(Supplementary Figure 15). In the same group, Siglec-Fhigh TANs had upregulated 
expression of iNOS, Arg-1, CD54 and PD-L1 (Figure 28), whereas the Siglec-F- TANs had 
higher iNOS, CD54 and PD-L1, but not Arg-1 (Figure 29). Finally, MSA IL-12 
administration led to a greater exclusion of Siglec-Fhigh TANs from the primary tumor bed 
(Figure 31). Although recent reports annotate a mature and pro-tumorigenic function to 
Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils in tumor bearing mice [96, 98, 151], their precise arsenal is not fully 
investigated yet. In our pursuit to explore the functional differences between the two 
populations, we compared the expressions of iNOS, Arg-1, CD54 and PD-L1. Our data 
collectively suggest that the Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils in the TME display a more activated and 
immunosuppressive phenotype (Supplementary Figure 18). 
During the past years, a specific neutrophil population has drawn a lot of attention due of its 
peculiarity. This population is characterized by the expression of molecules typically 
associated with APCs like CD40L, APRIL, MHC-II, CD11c and CCR7, and has be described 
in early stages of human lung cancer, possibly shaping the adaptive immune landscape [129, 
132-134, 161, 162]. In our model we also identified neutrophils expressing MHC-II in the 
primary tumor and the lungs (Supplementary Figure 3). Even though we did not detect an 
increase in the general APC-like neutrophil population in the TME, we did see an increase in 
the CD54+MHC-II+ neutrophils (Figure 27). The PD-L1+CD54+ APC-like TANs were also 
increased (Figure 27). The CD54+ APC-neutrophil hybrids had higher expression of PD-L1 
in response to all IL-12 formulations and Arg-1 only in response to MSA IL-12 (Figure 30). 
tSNE analysis in this dataset uncovered a rather interesting result. It seemed that 
administration of IL-12 formulations led to the emergence of an APC-like neutrophil 
population with pro-inflammatory properties (expression of iNOS, but not of Arg-1) 
==PAGE:74==
[69] 
 
(Supplementary Figure 13). Certain reports also suggest that NO produced by neutrophils 
impair the metastatic seeding in the lungs [142]. 
Similarly in the lungs, the total neutrophil frequencies were lower in the MSA IL-12 and 
TrIL-12 treated groups, compared to the untreated mice (Figure 32). Notwithstanding, the 
iNOS+ Nφ were increased in the TrIL-12 mice (Figure 32). Accordingly, the pro-tumorigenic 
Siglec-Fhigh population was reduced in response to both MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12, with the 
PD-L1+ cluster having an increase (Figure 33). On the other hand, the Siglec-F- Nφ were 
increased in the same groups, with the PD-L1+ subset also increasing (Figure 34). 
Subsequently, even though the Siglec-Fhigh Nφ were reduced, CD54 and PD-L1 expression 
was higher in the TrIL-12 group (Figure 35). This observation was also made for the Siglec-
F- Nφ cluster (Figure 36). Lastly, PD-L1 expression was significantly higher in the CD54+ 
APC-like neutrophils in response to TrIL-12 (Figure 37). Metastatic lesions were also 
assessed in response to the treatments, with TrIL-12 performing better compared to the other 
groups (Figure 38). 
Taken together, we suggest that at least in the 4T1 model, CD54 constitutes an activation 
marker rather than a N1 marker, and that IL-12 may be able to skew neutrophils towards a 
pro-inflammatory state.  
5.3 Effect of IL-12 regimens on NK cells 
After acquiring a better understanding on the IL-12/neutrophil relation, we began to evaluate 
the effect of IL-12 solely on NK cells, since we saw a delay in the tumor growth after MSA 
IL-12 and TrIL-12 administration (Figure 39), consistent with studies of IL-12 on sarcomas 
[149]. For this reason we used markers to estimate the functional aspect of NK cells such as 
IFN-g and GzmB, CD25 for activation, TIM-3 as an inhibitory receptor and CXCR3. 
Throughout our study we did not detect any CD27 expressing NK cells in the tumor or the 
lungs.  
CXCR3 is a chemokine receptor that is important for the mobilization of NK cells towards the 
tumor site [148]. In fact, certain reports highlight its pivotal role in NK cell migration as 
ablation of CXCR3 hampers their relocation in the TME, while in the case of myeloma, 
CXCR3 blockade facilitates the retention of NK cells in the BM [19, 163].  In our solid 
cancer model, even though we noticed an increase in CD11bhighCD27- NK cells in response to 
all IL-12 formats, we failed to see any outstanding changes in the CXCR3+ cells. Nonetheless, 
we did have more TINK in response to TrIL-12 compared to the other groups. In the lungs, 
however, TrIL-12 treatment led to an increase in the frequency of CXCR3+CD11b-CD27- 
(Figure 51) & CXCR3+CD11bintCD27- (Figure 53) NK cells, accompanied by higher 
expression of this chemokine receptor (Figure 52 & 54, respectively).  
Cytokine-mediated NK cell activation is known to induce certain changes on their phenotype 
[150]. Those changes are involved in their activation and consequent exhaustion. In order to 
estimate their level of activation and exhaustion in response to IL-12 we looked for CD25 and 
TIM-3. It has been shown that both of those receptors are upregulated I response to IL-12 
[136, 153, 164]. In the TME, CD25 expression was upregulated in response to all IL-12 
formulations, while TIM-3 expression was significantly higher in the MSA IL-12 and TrIL-12 
groups (Figure 41). Although there is a consensus that CD25 is an activation marker, the role 
of TIM-3 is no so clear. There are reports suggesting that TIM-3 is also an activation and 
maturation marker on NK cells [136, 141, 164, 165], apart from being an inhibitory receptor 
[148, 166]. Thus, TIM-3 has been proposed as a marker for a specific NK cell state called 
split-anergy. In this state NK cells become less cytotoxic, but they are still cytokine 
producing. Our findings are in accordance to the literature, since in the TME TIM-3+ NK cells 
produced more IFN-g than their TIM-3- counterparts (Figure 46). Regrettably, we could not 
detect any changes in their cytotoxic potential based on the expression of GzmB. Regarding 
GzmB expressing cells in the tumor bed, we detected a decrease in the GzmB+CD11b-CD27- 
NK cells (Figure 42), consistent with the lower expression of this cytotoxic mediator in the 
==PAGE:75==
[70] 
 
TrIL-12 treated mice (Figure 43). All in all, we detected higher IFN-g can CD25 expression 
in the CD11bhighCD27- NK cells compared to CD11b-CD27- and CD11bintCD27- NK cells 
(Supplementary Figure 20). 
As far as the lungs are concerned, we did observe a surge in the presence of NK cells in 
response to IL-12 treatments, with a specific reduction in the CD11b-CD27- and 
CD11bintCD27- NK cells, accompanied by an increase in the CD11bhighCD27- NK cell cluster 
(Figure 48). The percentages of IFN-g+ and GzmB+ NK cells were lower in the lungs of the 
TrIL-12 treated mice compared to the MSA IL-12 group (Figure 49). The CD11b-CD27- NK 
cells expressing CD25 and TIM-3 were more frequent in the TrIL-12 mice, whereas their 
IFN-g+ and GzmB+ counterparts were more in the MSA group (Figure 51). This trend was 
noticeable for their expression pattern as seen by the MFI calculation (Figure 52).  
Consistently, GzmB+CD11bintCD27- & IFN-g+CD11bintCD27- NK cells were more abundant 
in the MSA IL-12 group (Figure 53), with the respective expression of GzmB and IFN-g also 
being higher (Figure 54). It is noteworthy that the CD25+, IFN-g+  and TIM-3+ 
CD11bhighCD27- NK cells were reduced in the TrIL-12 and MSA IL-12 treated mice, albeit 
the GzmB+CD11bhighCD27- NK cells were increased solely in the MSA IL-12 group (Figure 
55). Surprisingly enough, IFN-g expression was higher in the untreated mice, while CD25 
and GzmB expression was increased in the MSA IL-12 group (Figure 56). Regarding the 
effect of the IL-12 compounds on metastasis control, all three of the constructs performed 
well in minimizing the metastatic lesions in the lungs, compared to the control (Figure 58).  
How can we, nonetheless, translate the differential expression of GzmB and IFN-g in the 
lungs? A possible explanation for the reduced frequency of IFN-g and GzmB expressing NK 
cells in the lungs could be the clear lungs. To be more specific, considering the anti-
tumorigenic effect of IFN-g [18, 167], we could assume that in presence of metastatic 
colonization there is a greater need for IFN-g and a cytotoxicity, while in absence of tumor 
lesions a sustained anti-tumor response is redundant. Another possible explanation is the late 
time-point. Since we were focused on the metastasis aspect of in this model, we did the flow 
analysis at a late time-point, after several cycles of IL-12 treatment. In this setting, NK cell 
responses (IFN-g, GzmB and CD25 expression) to recurrent doses of IL-12 might have been 
less robust, perhaps due to “desensitization” (in which case we missed the quick effect of IL-
12).   
5.4 It takes two to tango: NK cells and Neutrophils- the Final Chapter 
Admittedly, the relationship between NK cells and neutrophils is anything but simple. They 
are both cell of the innate immune system that depending on their environment, they exert 
different functions. In cancer, neutrophils generally are known as suppressors of NK cells [54, 
83, 86, 92, 100], while they play an important role for NK cell development and function in 
homeostatic conditions [168, 169]. On the other hand, NK cells also regulate neutrophils, and 
specifically they are able to induce apoptosis to the latter via NKp46 receptor [145]. NKp46+ 
NK cells are known to eliminate metastatic cells, an effect that could be mediated by TRAIL 
expression, and induce primary tumor editing in an IFN-g dependent manner [144, 170-173]. 
Neutrophils can demobilize NK cells by severing NKp46 from their cell surface [174]. The 
importance of the NKp46 receptor is highlighted from knock-out experiments, where in its 
absence mice succumb to increased metastatic dissemination [144, 170, 171]. The recognition 
of tumor cells by NKp46 expressing cells is mediated via specific ligands found on malignant 
cells [144, 171, 175]. Activation of NK cells with NKp46 killer engagers has shown a 
protective and cytotoxic potential against tumor cells [176]. Different NKp46 expressing NK 
cell subsets have been identified in human tumors, correlating with different prognosis [177, 
178]. 
Nevertheless, neutrophils should not be seen as the villain of the story, considering their pro-
inflammatory potential. Sun et al., among other research groups, demonstrated that 
neutrophils primed with IFN-g had lesser expression of pro-angiogenic mediators like MMP9, 
==PAGE:76==
[71] 
 
while they were able to activate NK cells, thus skewing the neutrophils towards a ppro-
inflammatory state [179, 180]. Certain other reports state the pro-inflammatory potency of 
neutrophils in different conditions as measured by the production of IFN-g [91, 181-183]. 
Human neutrophils were able to secrete IFN-g after stimulation with IL-12 [91]. 
Taken together all the above, we investigated the potential of neutrophils to produce IFN-g in 
our tumor model, and the role of NKp46 expressing NK cells in metastatic control. Finally, 
we aimed to illuminate the link between neutrophils, NK cells and tumor progression. To do 
so, we decided to deplete neutrophils via a depletion antibody targeting Ly6G [184]. The 
depletion of neutrophils worked really well, as we noticed a delay in the primary tumor 
growth. This trend was similar to the effect of i.v. administered TrIL-12. s.c. injected TrIL-12 
seemed to have an advantage in controlling tumor growth. Ultimately, the combination of 
aLy6G with the TrIL-12 demonstrated the strongest effect in detaining primary tumor 
progression (Figure 58). This evidence suggests the important role of TME orchestrated 
neutrophils in supporting cancer expansion. However, due to an adverse reaction to the 
antibody, we discontinued the injection and these groups were subsequently excluded from 
further analysis.  
Consistent with our previous results, TrIL-12 had a reducing effect on the Siglec-Fhigh pro-
tumorigenic neutrophils in the lungs of the 4T1 bearing mice, and an increasing trend in the 
APC-like neutrophils was also observed (Figure 59). IFN-g+ neutrophil subsets were indeed 
detected in the lungs of the mice, but their frequencies seemed to decline in the TrIL-12 
treated groups, compared to the HBSS group (Figure 60). This was also reflected in the 
decline IFN-g expression (Figure 61). In regards to the NK cell compartment, we saw an 
increasing patter in NK cell frequencies in response to TRIL-12, both i.v. and s.c., but a 
decreasing trend in the IFN-g+ cells in the NKp46+ cluster, but also in the other NK cell 
subsets (Figure 62). Moreover, the IFN-g+CD11b- and the NKp46+CD11b- subsets were 
increased in response to TrIL-12 via the i.v. administration (Figure 63), while the greatest 
IFN-g expression was seen in the CD11b- NK cells after the i.v. injection (Figure 64). 
Furthermore, the percentages of IFN-g+NKp46+CD11bint NK cells were decreased in both the 
TrIL-12 groups (Figure 65). Finally, the CD11bhighIFN-g+ NK cells were also reduced in the 
i.v. TrIL-12 mice compared to the untreated ones, which was also seen for the IFN-
g+NKp46+CD11bhigh cells (Figure 67).  
The colonogenic assay readout declared that both was of administration (i.v. and s.c.) of TrIL-
12 perform well in eliminating metastases in the lungs, with the first having a slight 
advantage over the latter (Figure 69). To explain this observation, we generated the 
neutrophil to NK cell ratios. In the s.c. group the ratio between the APC-like Nφ and the total 
NK cells was increased, reflecting the increase of the first and the slight reduction of the latter 
in this group compared to the untreated mice (Figure 70). Similar were the results in the 
APC-like Nφ to CD11bint NK cell ratio but for the i.v. group (Figure 71). The Siglec-Fhigh Nφ 
to CD11bhigh NK cell ratio was reduced, depicting the reduction of the pro-tumorigenic 
neutrophils from the lungs and the increase of the mature NK cells in response to TrIL-12 
(Figure 72). Finally, the ratio of Siglec-Fhigh to NKp46+CD11b- NK cells was also decreased 
consistent with the reduction and increase of the Siglec-Fhigh Nφ and NKp46+CD11b- NK 
cells, correspondingly (Figure 73). 
En masse, the above presented data suggests that the interplay between distinct cell subsets 
may influence the presence of metastatic lesions in the lungs. The increasing presence of 
APC-like Nφ may be an indication of a skewing towards a protective anti-tumorigenic 
alliance between neutrophils and NK cells. Additionally, the reduction in effector cells (IFN-g 
producing NK cells and neutrophils) post treatment might indicate that the TrIL-12 construct 
was effective in activating the immune system to clear any metastatic cells might have 
migrated to the lungs or might have tried to move towards them.  
==PAGE:77==
[72] 
 
5.5 Surviving the 4T1 cancer 
As a crescendo to this research, we decided to perform an efficacy study with a survival 
analysis where we tested all the treatments given throughout this project. MSA IL-12 and 
TrIL-12 have a clear effect in delaying the tumor growth more effectively than the other 
treatment options, with MSA IL-12 having a slight benefit in the long term (Figure 74).  
Apropos the survival analysis, no clear advantage was observed among the groups (Figure 
75). This was because the primary tumor was developed into a superficial wound, and from a 
humane endpoint the mice needed to be alleviated from their suffering.   
6. Concluding Remarks & Future Studies 
To epitomize, the scope of this research was to evaluate the effect of IL-12 based compounds 
as immunotherapeutic agents in the 4T1 syngeneic tumor model. The effect of IL-12 in tumor 
control has been studied by various researchers either in conjunction with modulating the 
lymphocytic or the myeloid compartment [111, 113, 115, 117, 153, 157, 158, 185, 186]. In 
fact few years ago an IL-12 based clinical trial on humans with metastatic solid tumors was 
initiated [187]. In our study we focused on the effect of IL-12 on NK cells and neutrophils, 
since these two cell populations have a rather interesting interplay [168, 188].  
Here we report that IL-12 based therapies exert a potent anti-metastatic effect in the 4T1 
cancer model, a mechanism probably mediated predominantly by NK cells. As far as the 
primary tumor growth is concerned, indeed IL-12 has a procrastinating effect.  Blocking of 
neutrophils seems to potentiate the effect of IL-12 immunotherapy in modulating tumor 
progression. Moreover, we uncovered that IL-12 may have a priming role on neutrophils, 
leading to the generation of a pro-inflammatory state, while simultaneously hampering the 
function of the pro-tumorigenic neutrophil cluster. Yet, the full functional palette of the 
Siglec-Fhigh pro-tumorigenic neutrophils needs to be studied. We showed that this cell 
population is more immunosuppressive in the TME, but the way it mediates and promotes 
metastasis still needs to be investigated. After identifying those mechanisms, we could 
develop more targeted approaches in treating metastasizing tumors by addressing different 
points throughout tumor progression.  
A possible way to move forward is the combination of the IL-12 based immunotherapy with 
some epigenetic modulators for instance PRMT inhibitors, as these compounds appeared to 
induce tumor cell apoptosis [189]. Simultaneous administration of immunotherapy activating 
the immune system with therapeutics that decreases tumor cell viability by interfering with 
gene expression through epigenetic alterations could be the next step in eliminating cancer. 
This is in hopes that synergizing immunogenic cell death with synthetic lethality will have a 
more potent effect in tumor clearance. Another option would be the exploitation of DC 
vaccines, as it has been demonstrated that they can prime T cells into an antitumor response 
and activate NK cells [190, 191]. Novel clinical trials are investigating the infusion of T cells 
harboring an IL-12 auto-secreting element (NCT04509726) and the adoptive transfer of 
specific CAR-NK cells in pancreatic cancer (NCT03941457) are ongoing. These platforms 
could potentially activate both innate (NK cells mediated) and adaptive (T cell mediated) 
immunity solid malignancies, while avoiding potential off-target effects. Another aspect 
could be the harvesting of the pro-inflammatory potential of neutrophils against cancer, e.g. 
by means of type I interferons or TGF-b inhibitors, since type I interferons are known to skew 
neutrophils to a pro-inflammatory state, while blocking of TGF-b has the same effect, not 
only on neutrophils but also on Tregs [85, 192]. Finally, considering the studies highlighting 
the role of exosomes in the anti-tumor response and the patients’ survival [193-195], 
characterizing the exosomal content and the interactome of tumor-derived and NK cell 
derived vesicles could unravel potential treatment options.

Supplementary Figure 1 Gating strategy for neutrophils in the TME & the lungs. Neutrophils were 
identified as Ly6G+F4/80- cells in the CD45+VD- (alive) cells. Within the general neutrophils, we gated the 
Siglec-F- and Siglec-Fhigh cells, and we also detected some Dextramer-AH-1+ neutrophils.  Siglec-F and 
Dextramer FMOs are shown in red.  
 
 
==PAGE:89==
[84] 
 
F M O
 
 
Supplementary Figure 2 NK cell phenotyping strategy in the TME & the lungs. CD3-CD49b+ NK cells were 
further characterized based on the CD11b expression as CD11b-, CD11b+/int and CD11bhigh cells. TIGIT 
expression was investigated within the three CD11b clusters as follows: CD11b-TIGIT+, CD11b+/intTIGIT+ 
& CD11bhighTIGIT+ NK cells. CD49b and TIGIT FMOs are shown in red.

Supplementary Figure 3 Phenotyping strategy of neutrophils in the TME & the lungs. Neutrophils were 
gated within the CD45+VD- (alive) cells as F4/80-Ly6G+. In the general neutrophil population we gated the 
APC-like neutrophils as MHC-II+ cells. The MHC-II+ neutrophils were further gated for CD54 and within 
this population we identified the iNOS+, Arg-1+ and PD-L1+ cells. Similarly, Siglec-F- and Siglec-Fhigh 
neutrophils were identified in the general Ly6G+F4/80- cells. Within the Siglec-F- neutrophils we gated for 
the iNOS+, CD54+, Arg-1+ and PD-L1+ neutrophils. Correspondingly, iNOS+, CD54+, Arg-1+ and PD-L1+ 
clusters were gated in the Siglec-Fhigh neutrophils.  
 
==PAGE:91==
[86] 
 
 
Supplementary Figure 4 Immune phenotyping of NK cells in the TME and the lungs. NK cells were 
identified as CD3-CD49b+ within the CD45+VD- (alive) cells. Then we gated the NK cell subsets based on the 
CD11b and CD27 markers as: CD11b-CD27-, CD11bintCD27- and CD11bhighCD27- NK cells. We did not see 
any CD27+ NK cells in the TME and the lungs. Finally, within the generic NK cells we gated those 
expressing GzmB (CD3-CD49b+GzmB+), IFN-g (CD3-CD49b+IFN-g+) and TIM-3 (CD3-CD49b+TIM-3+).  
FMOs for CD49b, IFN-g, GzmB and TIM-3 are displayed in red.

CD25+CD11b-CD27-, IFN-g+CD11b-CD27-, CXCR3+ & CXCR3- CD11b-CD27-, GzmB+CD11b-CD27- and 
TIM-3+CD11b-CD27- NK cells. FMOs for IFN-g, GzmB, CXCR3 & TIM-3 are displayed in red.

CD25+CD11bintCD27-, IFN-g+CD11bintCD27-, CXCR3+ & CXCR3- CD11bintCD27-, GzmB+CD11bintCD27- 
and TIM-3+CD11bintCD27- NK cells. FMOs for IFN-g, GzmB, CXCR3 & TIM-3 are displayed in red.

Supplementary Figure 8 Phenotypic identification of neutrophil subsets in the lungs. Within the CD45+VD- 
(alive) cells we identified neutrophils as Ly6G+CD115-. Then in this neutrophil population we gated the 
APC-like neutrophils as MHC-II+ cells and within this subset we gated some IFN-g+ APC-neutrophil 
hybrids. IFN-g+ neutrophils were also gated from the total Ly6G+CD115- population. Finally, we identified 
the Siglec-F- and Siglec-Fhigh clusters within the Ly6G+CD115- population. FMOs for Ly6G, IFN-g, MHC-II 
& Siglec-F are displayed in red.   
 
Supplementary Figure 9 Immunophenotyping strategy for NK cells in the lungs. NK cells were originally

CD11b: CD11b-, CD11bint and CD11bhigh NK cells. Moreover, within the CD3-CD49b+ NK cells we gated for 
==PAGE:95==

NKp46 and IFN-g and we identified a general CD3-CD49b+IFN-g+ and a CD3-CD49b+NKp46+IFN-g+ NK 
cell subset. The CD49b FMO is designated in red.

Supplementary Figure 14 Frequency of TAN in the primary tumor bed. A: percentage of total TAN within the 
immune cells. B: frequency of Arg-1 expressing TAN. C: frequency of iNOS expressing TAN. UT (n=8, red),

Supplementary Figure 18 Comparison in the expression of iNOS, Arg-1, CD54 and PD-L1 between Siglec-
Fhigh & Siglec-F- TAN among the groups. A: iNOS expression in the untreated group. B: iNOS expression in 
the native IL-12 treated mice. C: iNOS expression in the MSA IL-12 group. D: iNOS expression in the 
TrIL-12 receiving mice. E: Arg-1 expression in the untreated mice. F: Arg-1 expression in the native IL-12 
group. G: Arg-1 expression in the MSA IL-12 group. H: Arg-1 expression in the TrIL-12 treated mice. I: 
CD54 expression in the untreated mice. J:  CD54 expression in the native IL-12 treated mice. K: CD54 
expression in the MSA IL-12 treated group. L: CD54 expression in the TrIL-12 treated group. M: PD-L1 
expression in the untreated group. N: PD-L1 expression in the native IL-12 treated group. O: PD-L1 
expression in the MSA IL-12 receiving mice. P: PD-L1 expression in the TrIL-12 receiving mice. In each 
group: Siglec-Fhigh (n=8, khaki), Siglec-F- (n=8, purple). The expression is calculated as the mean 
fluorescence intensity (MFI). The data were analyzed by Kruskal-Wallis and Dunn’s multiple comparison 
tests in an a=0,05. The Arg-1, CD54 and PD-L1 expression in the TrIL-12 group was analyzed with Mann-
Whitney test, while for the rest a normal t-test was applied.

CD27-, CD11bintCD27- and CD11bhighCD27- NK cells subsets in the untreated mice. B: MFI comparison of

the native IL-12 group. C: MFI comparison of IFN-g, CD25 & GzmB among the CD11b-CD27-, 
CD11bintCD27- and CD11bhighCD27- NK cells subsets in the MSA IL-12 treated mice. D: MFI comparison of 
IFN-g, CD25 & GzmB among the CD11b-CD27-, CD11bintCD27- and CD11bhighCD27- NK cells subsets in 
the TrIL-12 receiving mice. CD11b-CD27- (n=8, light green). CD11bintCD27- (n=8, cerulean blue). 
CD11bhighCD27- (n=8, purple). One-way ANOVA and Tuckey’s multiple comparisons test was used for the 
analysis of the CD25 comparison in the UT and the MSA IL-12 groups, while for the rest of the data we 
used Kruskal-Wallis and Dunn’s multiple comparisons tests in an a=0,05.
